NOTICE OF INTENTION TO COMMENCE LARGE MINING OPERATIONS
The informational requirements in this form are based on provisions of the Mined Land
Reclamation Act, Title 40-8, Utah Code Annotated 1953, General Rules and Rules of Practice and
Procedures.
This form applies only to mining operations which disturb or will disturb more than five
acres in an incorporated area or ten acres in an unincorporated area at any given time.
"MINING OPERATIONS" means those activities conducted on the surface of the land for the exploration
for, development of, or extraction of a mineral deposit, including, but not limited to, surface mining and
the surface effects of underground and in situ mining, on-site transportation, concentrating, milling,
evaporation, and other primary processing.
"Mining operation" does not include: the extraction of sand, gravel, and rock aggregate; the extraction of
oil and gas as defined in Chapter 6, Title 40; the extraction of geothermal steam; smelting or refining
operations; off-site operations and transportation; or reconnaissance activities which will not cause
significant surface resource disturbance or involve the use of mechanized earth-moving equipment such
as bulldozers or backhoes.
Cultural Resources: To fulfill its obligations under Utah Code Annotated 9-8-404, the Division needs
cultural resource (archaeology) information. The amount and type of information required will depend on
the mine location, the history of previous disturbance, and other factors. Please contact the Division for
further information.

same or similar format should be used.

R647-4-105 - Maps, Drawings & Photographs
105.1 - Topographic base map, boundaries, pre-act disturbance
Base maps, boundaries and pre-act disturbances are illustrated in Figures 2 and 3. All mapped water
features were identified on 1:24,000 scale USGS maps (Figure 3) including any streams, springs, or
ponds in the project area.
The length of mapped water features in the Silver Mine disturbed area and 500 ft buffer. of the proposed

mining operation includes (Figure 6):
■ Canals/ditches = 973 ft.
• Perennial streams (Parleys Creek) = 1,688 ft.
• Ephemeral streams = 17,663 ft.
A reconnaissance-level field survey was completed to verify the location of mapped water features and
identify any unmapped surface water. Except for Parleys Creek drain, no water was identified in stream
channels. Stream channel features were identified at some locations, but no indication of surface water
was observed. Ponds, springs, seeps, and other forms of surface or ground water discharge were not
observed during the site survey in the proposed mining area or 500 ft. buffer.
Underground municipal water is transferred by pipeline between Mountain Dell Reservoir and Salt Lake
City's water service area. Parleys Creek enters an underground channel 0.25 miles downstream of
Mountain Dell Reservoir, prior to entering the subject property, and flows beneath the interstate for
approximately four miles to the mouth of Parleys Canyon. Some stormwater runoff collects in ephemeral
tributaries to Parley's Creek. Any water in these tributaries is diverted into stormwater drains located near
the highway passing through Parleys Canyon.
105.2 - Surface facilities map
The equipment area is illustrated in Figure 3.
105.3 — Drawings or Cross Sections (slopes, roads, pads, etc,)
The proposed mine design is illustrated in Figure 4 and cross section in Figure 5. Geologic and crosssectional maps are included in Figure 7 It is proposed to mine the property to an elevation of 5300 feet (ft)
Above Mean Sea Level (AMSL).
105.4 - Photographs
Photos of the site are included in Appendix A.
105.5 — Underground and Surface Mine Development Maps
The mine plan illustrated in Figure 4 and cross section in Figure 5 illustrates the final proposed mine
design. The mine will be mined in two phases which include 1) mining and grading the property flat on
the north side of the slope; and 2) establishing crushing equipment on the flattened floor and mining the
material to the south down to the profile illustrated in Figures 4 and 5. Assuming an average six foot tall,
nine foot wide berm, and 28,000 ft long berm (perimeter of the facility), the total topsoil stored within the
property berms is 25,000 cubic yards (cy). Overburden and topsoil removed during phase two will be
stored within the facility berms. Additional wash fines generated on site will be stored within the
operation and stockpile area illustrated in Figure 3 and once amended, would be used for reclamation
activities. Disturbed areas and timeline of disturbance are listed in Table 106.3-1.
It647-4-106 - Operation Plan
106.1 - Minerals mined
The mineral to be mined at the Tree Farm, LLC (Tree Farm) Project is limestone (calcite) of the Jurassic
Twin Creeks Limestone and other precious metals. The Twin Creek Limestone Formation consists of
multiple stacked limestone units. The rock ranges in color from light gray to gray or bluish gray. Bedding
ranges from thin (shaly) to medium-bedded and ledge forming. Texture of the limestone ranges from
shaly and silty micritic limestone, to more bioclastic and oolitic in places. Interbeds of sandstone,
siltstone, sandy limestone and silty limestone are mapped throughout the formation. There will also be a
small volume of sandstone from the Nugget Sandstone Formation. This sandstone is an eolian, fine
grained white quartz sandstone that exhibits a pale orange color in outcrop.

106.2 - Type of operations conducted, mining method, processing etc.
Tree Farm will conduct common practices typical with crushed and broken limestone mining and
quarrying. These practices include drilling, blasting, and use of dozers to rip and push the material down
the face of the slope. Once at the bottom of the slope, the rock is hauled to the primary feeder of the
aggregate plant. The aggregate plant is composed of a series of crushers, screens, and belts to adequately
size the rock into a viable product. All operations at the Silver Mine Pit will abide by the Mine Safety
and Health Administration (MSHA) and industry standards.
The mine is anticipated to be worked in stages from the north end leading south. Once the material is
mined near the northern property boundary to establish a pad for the crushing and screening equipment,
the excavation of material will continue to the south with the gradual development of benches forming the
slope illustrated in Figures 4 and 5. Mining of the head wall will create an overall slope of 1.0H:1.0V
from the toe to the crest as shown in Figure 5. Typical benches will be 50 feet high and 26 feet across,
with the intermediate slopes at 0.5 H:1.0 V.
Blasting and Crushing Operations
Operations would begin with drilling of blastholes. Drilling would be completed by use of air track drills,
or larger drill rigs to meet production requirements. The number of drill rigs used at a single time would
vary but is not expected to exceed more than four. Once drilled the hole would be filled with Ammonium
Nitrate Fuel Oil (ANFO) for blasting. Once blasted, the material would either be pushed down slope with
a dozer towards the primary jaw crusher for the initial sizing reduction.
The crushing and screening equipment that will be installed at the site will change through the life of the
pit but will generally conform to the following description.
A primary jaw crusher will break down the rock and which is then sorted in a vibratory screen. The
separated materials are fed through the rest of the plant. Road base material is removed early in the
process and stockpiled onsite. The other usable products are sent to a secondary cone crusher, where it is
further broken down in size. The rock then passes to a splitter bin that is used to ensure consistent feed
through the remainder of the plant. The material from the splitter bin is fed into a second vibratory
screen. Some end products are removed at this screen location and sent to stockpiles. Oversized rock is
sent to a tertiary crusher and screen where it is broken down and re-circulated to the second vibratory
screen, which sorts the material into sellable products. Stockpiles and equipment will be located in the
area illustrated in Figure 3.
A Utah Department of Air Quality (UDAQ) Approval Order (AO) will be obtained for the equipment on
site and kept up to date as the equipment list is changed. Other permits including water quality and oil
management and storage will be updated as the equipment is changed.
Stockpiles of engineering materials will be placed throughout the operation area illustrated in Figure 3.
Topsoil will primarily be stored along the perimeter of the site as a berm which will be seeded to prevent
soil erosion. Wash fines (post process material after washing crushed aggregates) and any topsoil not
stored within the berms, will be stored on site within the operational area illustrated in Figure 3. The wash
fines and topsoil stored in the operational area will be seeded to prevent soil erosion.
There are no deleterious or acid forming materials, nor shall any of these materials be left on site. If this
type of material becomes present, Tree Farm will take preventive actions to mitigate impacts.
106.3 - Estimated acreages disturbed, reclaimed, annually
Annual tonnage and change in disturbed acreage will depend largely on market conditions. Tree Farm

will disturb a maximum area of 634 acres on the property. The yearly estimated change in disturbed area
will range from 0.1 acres to up to 4 acres with the average being approximately 0.50 acres. The pit slope
benches will be reclaimed at the end of the mine life. Therefore, Tree Farm will bond to reclaim the entire
site. Table 106.3-1 lists the approximate areas to be disturbed over estimated timeframes. It is assumed
that all 634 acres will be disturbed at one time during the life of the mine.

106.4 - Nature of materials mined, waste and estimated tonnages
The limestone of the Jurassic Twin Creeks formation is the target material for extraction. Because
bedding, texture, purity and fracture density all fluctuate, waste percentages will do the same. It is
estimated that mine and processing waste (not including overburden), will average around 20% by
weight. This waste material can consist of a very fine limestone powder generated from breakdown due to
handling and crushing. This fine `dust' can be incorporated into various fill products as well as used for
pond liners and as a pH buffer for agricultural soils. Typically, local demand exceeds production of the
waste fines at other similar facilities in the area. Occasional rock types considered to be less competent,
more friable, or do not meet the specification requirements for high end aggregate (HMA and RMC) such
as mudstone, siltstone and sandstone will be encountered. This material can be incorporated in base
course material, granular borrow and fill. It is assumed 100% of the mined material will be useable. Tree
Farm is estimating an initial 500,000 tons of material (base rock) on an annual basis the first three to five
years of operation and increasing to more than 2,000,000 tons per year. The maximum allowable 12month production rate will be determined in the UDAQ approval order.
106.5 - Existing soil types, location, amount
Cirrus Ecological Solutions completed a soil survey, collected soil samples for analysis, completed a predisturbance wildlife and vegetation survey, and analyzed the potential impacts to each aspect. The report
is included in Appendix B and the findings included in this application.
Information on existing soil resources in the project area were acquired through the USDA Natural
Resource Conservation Service (NRCS) Web Soil Survey (WSS). The data shows nine soil units are in

the proposed mine area (Figure 8). This information describes existing soil conditions with sufficient
detail to meet the requirements for an Order 3 Soil Survey (Table 106.5-1). One soil sample was
collected from each soil unit across all parcels (Figure 8). Measures of pH and estimates of soil texture
were also determined for each soil sample (Table 106.5-2). Laboratory test results for salinity, and sodium
adsorption ratio (SAR), are included in Table 106.5-3, indicate that soils meet the highest category of soil
suitability (good) used to evaluate reclamation potential (DOGM 2000). Measurements of soil texture
(Table 106.5-2) and estimates of available water holding capacity based on texture (USU Extension 2001)
were also evaluated for reclamation potential. Except for Site #4 (Unit 151) all results are classified in the
good category for reclamation potential. The soil sample in Unit 151 had a clay texture which is in the
Fair and Poor categories for reclamation potential (DOGM 2000). However, soil survey results indicate
that Unit 151 has a gravelly loam texture.
Soil pits were excavated using a pick and shovel down to the lower boundary of the B horizon at each
sample location. Tools were cleaned between each sample site to prevent sample contamination. The
overall depth of each soil pit varied based on local conditions of the C horizon but were generally 20-36
inches deep. A composite sample was gathered from the A and B horizons by collecting subsamples at
roughly 4-inch intervals along the entire profile. Composite samples were stored in sterile plastic bags and
transported to the laboratory for testing within 24 hours of sample collection
A brief summary of properties for each soil unit in the proposed mine area is included below.
101 Agassiz-Rock outcrop complex. 30 to 70 percent slopes
Agassiz and similar soils make up 60 percent of the composition in this soil unit. Rock outcrop comprises
25 percent, and other minor components cover 15 percent of this unit. Soil depth is shallow, and a typical
profile has a depth to lithic bedrock of 14 inches. Soil texture is classified as very cobbly loam. Field
observations from Site 2 indicate sandy clay loam (Table 106.5-2). Soils are derived from limestone and
excessively drained. A total of 194.0 acres of the proposed mine area is mapped as soil unit 101.
136 Hades-Agassiz-Rock outcrop complex, 30 to 70 percent slopes
Hades and similar soils comprise 50 percent of this soil unit. Agassiz and Rock outcrop comprise 30 and
10 percent respectively, and other minor components cover 10 percent of this unit. Soil depth is typically
60 inches having an A and B horizon with a restrictive layer at a depth of 80 inches. The A horizon
extends to 18 inches below the surface, and the B horizon is present to a depth of 60 inches. The texture is
loam through the A horizon and clay loam in the B horizon. Field observations at Site 1 indicate soil
texture is primarily loam. Soils are derived from sandstone, quartzite, and shale and well drained. A total
of 153.0 acres of the proposed mine area is mapped as soil unit 136.
151 Lucky Star gravelly loam, 30 to 60 percent slopes
Lucky Star and similar soils comprise 85 percent of the soil unit, with other minor components covering
15 percent. Soil depth is typically 80 inches including an A, E, and B horizon. The A horizon extends to a
depth of 12 inches below the surface followed by an E horizon to a depth of 25 inches, and a B horizon
extending to 80 inches. Soil texture includes gravelly loam in the A horizon, very gravelly fine sandy
loam in the E horizon, and very cobbly sandy loam to very cobbly sandy clay loam in the B horizon. Field
observations of texture in Site 4 samples identified a predominately clay texture. Soils are derived from
conglomerate and sandstone and well drained. A total of 25.0 acres of the proposed mine area is mapped
as soil unit 151.
BCG Brad very rocky loamy sand, 40 to 80 percent slope
Brad and similar soils comprise 60 percent of the soil unit. Rock outcrop comprises 25 percent and other
minor components cover 15 percent of this unit. Depth to bedrock ranges from 14 to 24 inches throughout

the unit. The A horizon typically extends to 8 inches below the surface. No B horizon is described for this
soil unit. Soil texture in the A horizon is typically very cobbly loamy sand. The soil sample collected
from Site 3 was observed to have a loam texture with a high percentage of sand. Soils are derived from
sandstone and are excessively drained. A total of 24.5 acres is mapped in the proposed mine area as soil
unit BCG.
BEG Bradshaw-Agassiz association. steep
Bradshaw and similar soils comprise 55 percent of the soil unit. The remainder of the unit includes
Agassiz (35 percent) and minor components (10 percent). Depth to bedrock is over 80 inches. The typical
depth of the A horizon extends to 20 inches below the surface followed by the B horizon down to a depth
of 52 inches. Soil texture is very silty loam throughout this depth. Soil texture from Site 7 was observed
to be silt loam. Soils are derived from limestone, sandstone, and shale and well drained. A total of 112.1
acres is mapped in the proposed mine area as soil unit BEG.
DCG Deer Creek loam, 30 to 60 percent slopes
Deer Creek and similar soil comprise 90 percent of the soil unit and 10 percent minor components. The
depth to bedrock is over 80 inches. The A horizon extends to a depth of 11 inches, and the B horizon
continues to a depth of 45 inches. Soils on the surface are loam through the A horizon, transition to clay
loam down to a depth of 16 inches, and gravelly clay down to 45 inches. The observed texture at Site 6
was silt loam. Soils are derived from limestone, and well drained. A total of 33.1 acres is mapped in the
proposed mine area as soil unit DCG.
EMG Emigration very cobbly Ioam, 40 to 70 percent slopes
Emigration and similar soil comprise 95 percent of this soil unit. The remaining 5 percent consists of
minor components. The typical depth to unweathered bedrock ranges from 18 to 28 inches. The A horizon
is shallow from the surface to four inches deep, and there is no B horizon. The texture is very cobbly loam
and confirmed from soil samples taken at Site 5. Soils are derived from weathered limestone and
sandstone and are considered well drained. A total of 67.1 acres is mapped in the proposed mine area as
soil unit EMG.
HHF Harkers Soils. 6 to 40 percent slopes
Harkers and similar soils comprise 90 percent of the soil unit, respectively. The remaining 10 percent
consists of minor components. The typical depth to bedrock is greater than 80 inches. The A horizon
extends to a depth of 14 inches, and the B horizon continues to a depth of 58 inches. Soil texture in the A
horizon is cobbly loam, and the B horizon consists of gravelly clay loam transitioning to gravelly clay
with increasing depth. Visual observations of soil samples at Site 8 identified primarily loam soil texture.
Soils are derived from limestone, sandstone, and shale and well drained. A total of 1.7 acres is mapped in
the proposed mine area as soil unit HHF.
RO Rock Land
Rock Land is considered a miscellaneous area in the soil survey with little to no soil associated with the
soil unit. Observations during the site visit confirmed the area is primarily rock scree consisting of cobble
to boulder-sized rock. Samples were taken at Site 9 to describe soil resource conditions. Soil texture at
this site was consistent with loam soil. A total of 7.1 acres in the proposed mine area is mapped as soil
unit RO.

106.6 - Plan for protecting & re-depositing soils
Tree Farm will comply with rules listed in section R-647, as they pertain to the Facility. Topsoil removal
will be separated from mineable material. All stockpiled or bermed topsoil will be planted/seeded with
native plants to prevent deterioration from wind or water erosion. As specified in R647-4-107.5, a
sufficient quantity of suitable soils, 331,540 cubic yards as described in Section 110.5, will be removed
and stored in a stable condition where practical so as to be available for reclamation. Topsoil that is not
stored within the facility berms and wash fines generated from washing aggregate will be stored on site
and used for reclamation as a soil cover. Areas of the mine site will be covered with the amended soils if
the area is completely mined and no longer used for operational purposes. To prevent erosion and

degradation, the stockpiled topsoil will be vegetated using the seed mix listed in Table 110.5-1. A test pad
will be created of the amended wash fines within the stormwater detention pond to determine the
effectiveness of using the amended wash fines as a topsoil. The vegetation grown within the pond will
increase the contaminate reduction efficiency of the pond.
The size and nature of the site will not allow for concurrent reclamation, though as stated above, any
opportunity to reclaim unused portions of the site will be implemented. As proposed, all acreage to be
disturbed will be covered in topsoil and re-vegetated when mining permanently ceases at the site. The
bond amount will be enough to reclaim the entire mine site. Reclamation activities will include placement
of 6 inches of topsoil and planting with the seed mix listed in Table 110.5-1.
106.7 - Existing vegetation - species and amount
Descriptions of the plant communities in the overall project area were developed by surveying the
different plant communities. Aerial imagery was used to identify different plant communities in the
project area. Vegetation cover transects were established in each plant community. A total of 10, 100meter long transects were placed across all vegetation types throughout the overall survey area (Figure 9).
Cover was read at 1-meter increments. Aerial canopy cover was recorded for each species intersected by
the point. If multiple species of vegetation were intersected by the point, as in overstory and understory
layers, all species were recorded. Thus, total aerial canopy cover may sum to greater than 100 percent.
However, aerial canopy cover percentages shown in Table 106.7-1 have been adjusted to show canopy
cover with no overlap. Total aerial canopy cover in Table 106.7-1 includes overlapping vegetation layers
for each individual plant species identified (Appendix B). In addition, ground cover was also recorded.
Categories of groundcover include bare ground, litter, rock, and basal vegetation. Total ground cover
sums to 100 percent (Table 106.7-1). Canopy cover and ground cover are independent metrics.
Field survey efforts and aerial imagery review were used to determine plant communities within the
survey area. Table 106.7-1 summarizes the five plant communities that occur in the survey area. These
include deciduous woodlands, conifer forest, mountain shrub rock outcrop, dry short forb, and grass forb.
Each community is described below. Table 106.7-2 lists the species that were identified during the field
surveys across all transects. Appendix B provides detailed transect summary data for each transect. Table
106.7-3 summarizes the acreage of each plant community across the parcels which include the Silver
Mine (Figure 11). No endangered or threatened species were observed within the subject property during
the field survey.
Deciduous Woodlands
Deciduous woodlands is the most common plant community occurring in the project area (Figure 11).
The cover and composition within the deciduous woodlands plant community varies depending on slope,
aspect, and elevation. Six deciduous woodlands subtypes were identified and sampled (Table 106.7-1).
Gambel oak is the dominant species. Co-dominant or subdominant tree/shrub species include big-tooth
maple, snowberry, chokecherry, and serviceberry. Oregon grape was also subdominant in some locations.
Herbaceous understory composition varied between subtypes. In some subtypes, herbaceous understory
was very limited. Other sites included a more prominent herbaceous understory composed of such species
as arrowleaf balsamroot, mule's ear, Wasatch desert parsley, and mountain brome. Appendix B provides a
list of species and corresponding cover for species comprising the deciduous woodlands plant
community.
Conifer Forest
Conifer forest occurs on north-east facing aspects. It is dominated by subalpine fir overstory with a
ninebark understory. Other important shrubs include chokecherry and serviceberry. Herbaceous
understory is limited. The most common species included heart-leaf arnica and elk sedge. Litter provides
high ground cover for this community type. Appendix B provides a list of species and corresponding

cover for species comprising the conifer forest community type.
Mountain Shrub-Rock Outcrop
Mountain shrub-rock outcrop occurs on steep, rocky slopes. It is shrub-dominated. Gambel oak is the
most common shrub, followed by rockspirea, buckwheat, curl-leaf, mahogany, and sagebrush. Litter is
limited in this community. Juniper occurs as scattered occurrences in the community type as well.
Appendix B provides a list of species and corresponding cover for species comprising the mountain
shrub-rock outcrop community type.
Dry Short Farb
The dry short forb community type occurs on dry south-facing slopes. Overall vegetation cover is low,
dominated by orange agoseris, and arrowleaf balsamroot. Appendix B provides a list of species and
corresponding cover for species comprising the dry short forb community type.
Grass Forb
The grass-forb community type occurs on northerly aspects. Dominate species include bluebunch
wheatgrass, mountain brome, and smooth brome, arrowleaf balsamroot, mules ear, and buckwheat.
Snowberry and ninebark are the dominant shrubs that occur in this community. Appendix B provides a
list of species and corresponding cover for species comprising the grass-forb community type.

106.8 - Depth to groundwater, extent of overburden, geology
Groundwater
AECOM was tasked to complete a hydrogeology study of the site which did not include a field
investigation component. The report is included in Appendix C and summarized below.
Recharge to the shallow aquifer feeding Parleys Creek from the study area occurs when water derived
from snowmelt, soaks through the thin site soils and fractured bedrock covering the topographicallyelevated drainage. Water percolates through preferential pathways in the fractured bedrock in the upper
portion of the site through the Thaynes Limestone and through the lower portion of the site through the
Nugget Sandstone. Infiltrated water in the Thaynes and Nugget Formations is stored in fractures. As
storage from infiltration increases hydraulic head also increases, driving flow through fractures
topographically down slope. The absence of springs and seeps within the study area indicates that the
occurrence of groundwater is likely deeper than groundwater in the adjacent drainage basin to the east,
the Mount Aire Stream area. The siltstones and mudstones associated with the Upper and Mahogany
Members of the Ankareh Formation likely limits or retards groundwater flow within the study area.
Overburden
Overburden at the site consists of topsoil deposited in the valleys of the site. The steep slopes and ridge
tops contain no collectable amount of over burden and will be mixed into the surrounding materials as
mineable material. Approximately 100,000 cy of topsoil is estimated to be present at the site. Some of this
material will be used as topsoil for reclaiming the site once mining is completed.

106.8.1: Geologic Setting
Regional Setting
Utah is subdivided into three major physiographic and tectonic provinces: the Basin and Range, Middle
Rocky Mountains, and Colorado Plateau. The site is located near the boundary of the Middle Rocky
Mountain, and Basin and Range Physiographic Provinces. The Middle Rocky Mountain province in
northeastern Utah consists of mountainous terrain, stream valleys and alluvial basins. It includes the
Wasatch Mountain Range, comprised mainly of Pre-Cambrian, Paleozoic and Mesozoic sedimentary and
metamorphic rocks, as well as sparse Tertiary igneous and volcanic rocks. The Basin and Range Province
contains numerous tilted, north-south oriented mountain ranges separated by sediment filled basins. This
province is bounded in the east by the Wasatch Range, and to the west by the steep, fault blocked eastern
Sierra Nevada Range.
The Tree Farm Project is approximately 3.3 miles east of the main segment of the Wasatch Fault Zone
(WFZ) at the mouth of Parley's Canyon. The WFZ represents the theoretical provincial boundary of the
Basin and Range and Middle Rocky Mountain. The Project Area is in a `transition zone' between the
Basin and Range and Middle Rocky Mountain Physiographic Provinces.
Site Specific
The subject property specifically resides in the western portion of Parley's Canyon in the Middle Rocky
Mountain Physiographic Province. Elevations of the properties fall within an approximate range of 5,350
feet above mean sea level (AMSL) to over 7,500 feet AMSL. It lies on the boundary of the Sugar House
and Mount Aire 7.5" Geologic Quadrangle. The terrain was mapped and described in detail by Adam P
McKean of the Utah Geologic Survey in 2020. Rocks in this area include the Triassic Ankareh
Formations, the Jurassic Nugget Sandstone, the Jurassic Twin Creek Limestone with sparse young
Quaternary alluvium in places. The Ankareh Formation consists of the Mahogany Member and the Gartra
Grit Member. The Mahogany Member consists mainly of reddish-brown to pinkish-brown fine-grained
sandstone, siltstone, and a purple-colored mudstone. The Gartra Grit Member is composed of whitish,
pinkish white sandstone that is massive, coarse grained and silica cemented (Ashland et. Al 1996). The
younger Jurassic rocks consist of the Nugget Sandstone and the Twin Creek Limestone. The Nugget is
orange to pinkish colored, fine to medium grained sandstone that exhibits cross bedding.
The target formation for mineral extraction is the Jurassic Twin Creek Limestone Formation. This
formation is very thick, approximately 2,600 feet thick in this area. The Twin Creek Formation makes up
100% of the northern property, and over 95% of the southern property project area (McKean 2020).
Extensive compressional folding and faulting that took place between the Jurassic and early Tertiary have
produced complex structural relationships and numerous unconformities within the subject area. The
dominant structure in this region is the Mount Raymond Thrust. Continued folding of the thrust plane has
created a series of first and second phase crustal shortening events resulting in a series of small to large
scale anticlines and synclines with fold axes plunging east-northeast (Crittenden et al. 1966; Bryant,
1990). Parley's Canyon is a syncline. Smaller scale folds are evident within the Twin Creek Formation on
the north side of the interstate exposed in the current quarry operation, and immediately east. The Tree
Fam Project is located along the southern wing of the Parley's Canyon Syncline.
106.9 - Location & size of ore, waste, tailings, ponds
There will be no waste stockpiles, as the current market supports all materials produced from the facility.
There are no tailings or treatment ponds on site and therefore no discharges from such sources of
contamination. Overburden and topsoil will be placed as berms along the mine boundary illustrated in
Figure 3.

Retention basin/sedimentation pond and ephemeral streams
To ensure that the basins can adequately retain the 10-year 24-hour storm even from compacted soils
during operation a hydrology analysis was completed by Horrocks Engineering and is included in
Appendix D. The mine floor will be sloped towards the mine wall to retain as much stormwater on site as
possible. Any stormwater that flows to the north will be collected in constructed V notch streams and
discharged into the detention basin on the north end of the site (Figure 4). Channel and pond calculations
are included in Appendix D.

R647-4-107 — Operation Practices
During operations, the operator shall conform to the following practices:
• Public Safety and Welfare - The operator shall minimize hazards to the public safety and welfare
during operations. Methods to minimize hazards shall include but not be limited to:
o The closing or guarding of shafts and tunnels to prevent unauthorized or accidental entry
in accordance with MSHA regulations;
o The disposal of trash, scrap metal and wood, and extraneous debris;
o The plugging or capping of drill, core, or other exploratory holes as set forth in Rule
R647-4-108;
o The posting of appropriate warning signs in locations where public access to operations is
readily available;
o The construction of berms, fences and/or barriers above highwalls or other excavations
when required by the Division.
• Drainages - If natural channels are to be affected by the mining operation, then the operator shall
take appropriate measures to avoid or minimize environmental damage.
• Erosion Control - Operations shall be conducted in a manner such that sediment from disturbed
areas is adequately controlled. The degree of erosion control shall be appropriate for the sitespecific and regional conditions of topography, soil, drainage, water quality or other
characteristics.
• Deleterious Materials - All deleterious or potentially deleterious material shall be safely removed
from the site or kept in an isolated condition such that adverse environmental effects are
eliminated or controlled.
• Soils - Suitable soil material shall be removed and stored in a stable condition where practical so
as to be available for reclamation.
Areas which have been disturbed but are not routinely or currently utilized shall be kept in a safe,
environmentally stable condition.

R647-4-108 - Hole Plugging Requirements
All drill holes will be plugged in accordance with Rule R647-4-108.
Hole Plugging Methods:
• Drill holes shall be properly plugged as soon as practical and shall not be left unplugged for more
than 30 days without prior approval by DOGM.

■

•

•

■

•
•

•

Dry holes and non-artesian holes that do not produce significant amounts of water may be
temporarily plugged with a surface cap to enable Tree Farm to re-enter the hole for the duration
of set operations.
Surface plugging of drill holes outside the actual mine area shall be accomplished by setting a
nonmetallic permaplug at a minimum of five (5) feet below the surface or returning the cuttings
to the hole and tamping the returned cuttings to within five (5) feet of ground level. The hole
above the permaplug or cuttings will be filled with a cement plug. If cemented casing is to be left
in place, a concrete surface plug may not be required as a permanent cap is secured on top of the
casing.
Drill holes that encounter water, oil, gas, or other potential migratory substances and are 2.5
inches or greater in surface diameter will be plugged in the subsurface to prevent the migration of
fluid from one stratum to another. If water is encountered, plugging shall be accomplished as
outlined below.
If artesian flow (i.e. water flowing to the surface from the hole) is encountered during or upon
cessation of drilling, a cement plug will be placed to prevent water from flowing between
geologic formation and at the surface. The cement will consist of API Class A or H cement, with
additives as needed, and will weigh at least 13.5 lbs/gal. It will be placed under the supervision
of a person qualified in proper drill hole cementing or artisan flow.
Artesian bore holes will be plugged as described prior to removal of drilling equipment from the
well site.
If the surface owner of the land affected desire to convert an artesian drill hole into a producing
and/or monitor well, the landowner will provide written notification to DOGM accepting
responsibility for the ultimate plugging of the drill hole.
Holes that encounter significant amounts of non-artesian water shall be plugged by: 1) placing a
50-ft cement plug immediately above and below the aquifer(s) or filling from the bottom up
(through the drill casing) with a high grade bentonite/water slurry mixture. The slurry shall have
a Marsh Funnel viscosity of at least 50 seconds per quart prior to the adding of any cuttings.

R647-4-109 - Impact Assessment
109.1 - Impacts to surface & groundwater systems
109.1.1 Surface Water
The project area is in Salt Lake County and has an average annual rainfall of 26 inches. There are
ephemeral streams that run through the site (Illustrated in Figure 6). The grade is generally steep slopes to
the north towards Parleys Creek.
Treatment of stormwater before discharging to Parleys Creek will primarily be by use of an onsite
detention/retention pond for sediment and nutrient removal. The pond will be constructed on the north
end of the site and will have the capacity to retain the 24-hour, 10-year storm event. The required storage
volume in the pond will increase as the pit is developed due to increases in the runoff potential of the
excavated mine face and floor. (2.9 acre-ft at complete site disturbance). As the pit develops, the floor of
the pit will be graded toward the back of the pit to create a larger storage area. The pond could discharge
the historic flow rate of 4 cfs to reduce needed volume or drain time in the pond.
To increase the sediment and nutrient load removal efficiency and soil hydrologic conductivity, the dry
pond will be planted using the seed mix listed in Table 110.5-1. Irrigation water will be applied to the
pond area to ensure vegetation vitality and longevity during summer and fall months. Hydraulic
calculations for the offsite drainage area, the onsite pond and the ephemeral channel designs are included
in Appendix D.

Tree Farm will prepare and implement a Storm Water Pollution Prevention Plan (SWPPP) in accordance
with rules and regulations set forth by the DWQ (Appendix E). The facility will submit for coverage
under the General Industrial Stormwater Permit Subsector J (mineral mining). At all times during the
operation, the SWPPP will be up to date with current topography, best management practices (BMPs),
other applicable information maintained in the SWPPP. Due to the nature and activities in the Project
Area, the SWPPP will be constantly changed and is regularly updated with site conditions. The SWPPP
and associated BMPs for the site will help ensure there are no adverse effects on the surface water system.
Groundwater
The nature of aggregate operations does not adversely affect ground water systems. However, the major
activities on the mine property that could impact groundwater if residues were to reach the resources are:
1) blasting; 2) presence of diesel fuel, lubricants, etc. used in the heavy equipment used at the mine, 3)
human wastes, which are processed through chemical toilets, which are serviced regularly. In summary:
•

•
•

Good housekeeping practices and careful operating procedures are used to minimize fuel and
lubricant spills. Fuel and lubricants are stored in above ground portable tanks that are double lined
to protect against spills.
The quantities of blasting materials used create negligible quantities of nitrates that, in the unlikely
event that they reached the groundwater would be well below water quality limits.
It is anticipated that the mine operation excavation necessary for material product and resultant
quarry floor bottom elevation of about 5,300' will not intercept groundwater. There will be about
feet in vertical separation between the quarrying activities and the potential to encounter
groundwater.

In addition, to prevent contamination with petroleum-based products used at the site for mineral
extraction, a Spill Prevention Control and Countermeasure (SPCC) plan will be created to protect soil and
groundwater from petroleum-based products used at the site.
109.2 - Impacts to threatened & endangered wildlife/habitat
Cirrus Ecological Solutions completed a wildlife and habitat survey of the project area and the report is
included in Appendix B. The report contents are also included below.
The project area falls within the potential range of two threatened or endangered species, the Canada lynx
(Lynx canadensis; threatened) and the western yellow-billed cuckoo (Coccyzus americanus; threatened).
Threatened and Endangered Species
Canada Lynx
Canada lynx require high-elevation coniferous forests with a dense, complex understory for foraging and
denning. A typical home range for Canada lynx is large (often greater than 3,700 acres) and consists of a
matrix of high-quality foraging and denning habitat as well as lower-quality habitat that is used to move
among higher-quality areas (Ulev 2007). The Silver Mine project area is predominantly shrubs and
grassland, with forested habitat making up approximately 10 percent (64.7 acres) of the project area.
Should a Canada lynx home range overlap the project area, the area would likely provide movement
habitat as opposed to high-quality foraging or denning habitat, given its limited understory complexity
and size.
The large home range, elusive nature, and wide-ranging movement patterns of Canada lynx make
surveying for this species difficult. Therefore, no surveys were completed for this species. Based on the
poor quality of the habitat present and the distance from known lynx populations, it is assumed that this
species would very rarely utilize the project area.

The Silver Mine would impact potential Canada lynx habitat. However, this habitat is not likely highquality foraging nor breeding habitat for Canada lynx that may be utilizing the area. Therefore, impacts
on Canada lynx would be limited to lower-quality matrix habitat, and would likely not result in any
effects, and certainly no population-level effects.
Western Yellow-billed Cuckoo
The Silver Mine project area does not provide habitat for western yellow-billed cuckoos. The project area
does not have riparian corridors with dense, continuous cottonwoods, willows, and shrubs required by
yellow-billed cuckoos for foraging and breeding (UDWR 2020). Furthermore, western yellow-billed
cuckoos are mainly known to occupy only two areas in Utah with consistency: north of the town of Green
River, and near the Ouray National Wildlife Refuge (UDWR 2020). The project area is not located
between these two core areas, and thus would not provide temporary movement habitat should individuals
move between the two areas.
Given the lack of habitat in the project areas, and distance from known populations, it is assumed this
species is not present. Therefore, no surveys were completed for this species. No impacts are expected.
Species of Special Concern
Species of special concern were selected from the Utah Sensitive Species list (UDWR 2017). While the
Utah Sensitive Species list is no longer maintained by the UDWR, it represents the best available list of
species of special concern in the area. Several migratory bird species were included that the U. S. Fish
and Wildlife Service has required analysis for on other projects in similar areas and habitats.
Prior to field surveys, aerial imagery was examined to identify broad habitat classes present in the project
area to eliminate any species from consideration that would not be present based on large-scale habitat
needs. Table 7 lists the species whose broad habitat needs were identified through aerial imagery. Initial
habitat surveys were then completed in the field to identify whether suitable habitat was available for each
species given their habitat needs within each broad habitat class.
Based on the results of the habitat surveys (Table 7), the only species of concern that could be impacted
by the Silver Mine project are northern goshawk, bald eagle, ferruginous hawk, short-eared owl, Lewis's
woodpecker, migratory birds, and the three species of bats.
Northern Goshawk
Suitable foraging habitat for northern goshawk (Accipiter gentiles) can be highly variable, with
individuals utilizing a wide range of habitat types. Breeding habitat for northern goshawk is more
specific, with nests often located in areas with dense canopy cover typical of older forests (USFS 2019).
The Silver Mine project area is predominantly shrubs and grassland, with forested habitat making up
approximately 10 percent (64.7 acres) of the project area. Based on habitat models, the project area is
located adjacent to high quality northern goshawk habitat, which is defined as providing both high quality
foraging and nesting habitat (Graham et al. 1999). The project area itself appears to provide higher quality
foraging habitat, but lower quality breeding habitat (Graham et al. 1999).
Following the dawn acoustic protocol (USFS 2006), remote acoustic recorders were deployed near
potentially suitable forested habitat in the project area in April of 2021. Recorders were deployed twice in
the same locations, separated by two weeks to ensure adequate temporal coverage. The recordings were
subsequently analyzed for northern goshawk calls by viewing and listening to the spectrograms in
Wildlife Acoustics Kaleidoscope Pro software. No northern goshawks were detected at any of the
recorders.
Given the diversity of habitat that northern goshawks use for foraging, and proximity to high quality
nesting habitat, it is possible that northern goshawks may use the project area for foraging. Therefore,
some foraging habitat may be impacted. Given the lack of detections within the forested habitat, lack of
breeding habitat, and larger home ranges of northern goshawks, impacts to northern goshawks would be
limited, and would not result in any population-level effects.

Bald Eagle
Bald eagles (Haliaeetus leucocephalus) build large stick nests in tall trees along waterways, and forage
great distances in search of food, including carrion. The project area does not provide any nesting habitat
or high-quality foraging habitat for bald eagles. However, bald eagles may occasionally utilize the project
area in search of carrion. Due to the likely sporadic use of the project area, no surveys were completed for
bald eagles. Bald eagles are assumed to occasionally be present in the project area. Given the likely
limited presence in the project area and lack of suitable breeding habitat, no impacts are expected.
Ferruginous Hawk
Suitable habitat for ferruginous hawks (Buteo regalis) includes dry, open habitat with scattered trees and
woodlands. They forage in open grassland and shrubland habitat where they can find abundant prey
species, such as rabbits, ground squirrels and prairie dogs. Nesting habitat for ferruginous hawks can vary
widely based on habitat availability. If trees are available, ferruginous hawks will likely place their nest in
a tree. However, they also nest on the ground, along short cliff edges, or on top of powerline poles (Tesky
1994a).
Given the generalist nature of their foraging and nesting needs, surveys focused on identifying any stick
nests in the project area that may be used by ferruginous hawks. Remote aerial surveys were completed
with a UAV in June of 2021. These surveys were completed in a pattern that covered all suitable habitat
in the project area. No large stick nests were identified that might be attributed to ferruginous hawks. The
stick nests located on the cliffs in the northeastern-most parcel (Figure 10) could potentially have been
constructed by ferruginous hawks, but this species typically prefers trees when available.
This species may still utilize the project area for foraging, and the Silver Mine would impact foraging
habitat. Given the lack of nests identified in the area, and wide foraging range of this species, impacts to
ferruginous hawks would be limited, and would not result in any population-level effects.
Short-eared Owl
Suitable foraging habitat for short-eared owls (Asioflammeus) is typically open areas with short
vegetation, which is common throughout many parcels. Nests are built on the ground in tall grasses or
shrubs (Wiggins et al. 2020).
Point counts were conducted in suitable habitat in April and May of 2021 to identify any short-eared owls
that may be utilizing the area. Twelve point-counts were completed, distributed in habitats throughout the
project area. No short-eared owls were noted during the point count surveys.
It is possible short-eared owls may still utilize the area for foraging, and the Silver Mine would impact
foraging habitat. Given the lack of detections and wide availability of suitable habitat in the surrounding
area, impacts would be limited, and would not result in any population-level effects.
Lewis's Woodpecker
Suitable habitat for Lewis's woodpeckers (Melanerpes lewis) includes riparian woodlands, recently
burned forested habitat and ponderosa pine forests. Lewis's woodpeckers do not excavate for insects, as
many other woodpeckers do. Instead, they catch insects from the air during short flights. As such, they
often occupy areas with open canopy and low tree density to allow room for foraging. Nests are placed in
trees that are decayed and easy to excavate (Abele et al. 2004). The Silver Mine project area is
predominantly shrubs and grassland, with forested habitat making up approximately 10 percent (64.7
acres) of the project area.
Habitat surveys were completed in June of 2021 to determine if suitable habitat for Lewis's woodpeckers
was present in any of the parcels. No suitable habitat was identified for Lewis's woodpeckers in any of
the parcels. Nevertheless, surveys for this species were completed during the point-counts for migratory
birds. No Lewis's woodpeckers were detected. Given the lack of suitable habitat and the lack of
detections, it is assumed that this species is not present. No impacts are expected.

American three-toed Woodpecker
Suitable habitat for American three-toed woodpeckers (Picoides dorsalis) includes old-growth stands,
areas that have been recently burned, or areas that have numerous snags present. Densities of three-toed
woodpeckers appears to be higher in areas where there has been a recent fire or beetle infestation. They
prefer to nest in mature conifer forests that have had some sort of disturbance (UDWR 2021b).
Habitat surveys were completed in June of 2021 to determine if suitable habitat for three-toed
woodpeckers was present in any of the parcels. No suitable habitat was identified for three-toed
woodpeckers in any of the parcels. Nevertheless, surveys for this species were completed during the
point-counts for migratory birds. No American three-toed woodpeckers were detected. Given the lack of
suitable habitat and the lack of detections, it is assumed that this species is not present. No impacts are
expected.
Migratory Birds
Eight migratory birds of interest were identified as potentially using the project area (Brewer's sparrow
[Spizella breweri], long-billed curlew [Numenius americanus], olive-sided flycatcher [Contopus cooperi],
rufous hummingbird [Selasphorus rufus], Virginia's warbler [Vermivora virginiae], willow flycatcher
[Empidonax traillii], golden eagle [Aquila chrysaetosJ, and long-eared owl [Asio otus]). These species
utilize a wide range of habitat types for foraging and breeding that are found within the project area,
including forested habitat, shrubby habitat, and grasslands. Golden eagles and long-eared owls are
evaluated in separate sections below.
Due to the wide variety of habitat types that these species use, surveys were conducted to identify all
birds that might be present in each of the parcels. Point counts were conducted in April and May of 2021
to identify any of the listed species that might be utilizing the area. Twelve point-counts were completed,
distributed in habitats throughout the project area. While many non-target bird species were observed
utilizing the parcels, none of the species of concern were detected.
It is possible these species utilize the area, as suitable habitat is present in the parcels. The Silver Mine
would disturb suitable habitat. Given the lack of detections and wide availability of suitable habitat in the
surrounding area, impacts would be limited, and would not result in any population-level effects.
Golden Eagle
Golden eagles utilize a wide variety of habitats, but primarily open habitat in hilly and mountainous areas.
Golden eagles can forage great distances in search of prey, including carrion. Nesting habitat is typically
cliff ledges or large trees, generally near grassland habitat. Nests are built of sticks and are very large,
often several feet wide and deep (Tesky 1994b).
Given the generalist nature of their foraging habitat, surveys were focused on identifying any suitable
breeding habitat or nests. There are areas within the project boundary that represent suitable golden eagle
nesting sites. These areas were surveyed by spotting scope and UAV in June of 2021. Figures 10 and 11
show two potential golden eagle nests on cliffs in the project boundary. It is unknown how recently these
nests have been used, and while the construction and location of these nests indicates likely construction
by golden eagles, none were observed. Given the presence of these nests, it is likely that golden eagles are
utilizing, or have utilized, the project area as breeding and foraging habitat. Both nests are on the same
band of cliffs and are likely alternate nests used in the past by the same breeding pair.
Given the presence of likely golden eagle nests in the project area, but the unknown status of their recent
use, it is possible that one nesting pair of golden eagles could be impacted by the Silver Mine Project.
Should this occur, the breeding pair would need to use an alternate nest site as the existing nest site would
be destroyed by mining activities. Fortunately, there is ample cliff nesting habitat available within half a
mile or less to the east, west, and south. Due to the presence of alternate nesting sites outside of the
project area, it is unlikely that this project would result in any population-level effects.

Long-eared Owl
Long-eared owls breed in forested habitat where they utilize stick nests built by other birds, such as
magpies and crows. Suitable breeding habitat is always located adjacent to openings, meadows and fields
that provide suitable foraging habitat (Marks et al. 2020). The Silver Mine project area is predominantly
shrubs and grassland, with forested habitat making up approximately 10 percent (64.7 acres) of the
project area.
Broadcast surveys were completed in suitable long-eared owl habitat in April of 2021. No long-eared
owls were detected during the broadcast surveys.
It is possible that this species may still utilize the project area for foraging, and the Silver Mine would
impact foraging habitat for long-eared owls. Given the lack of detections and wide availability of suitable
habitat in the surrounding area, impacts would be limited, and would not result in any population-level
effects.
Fringed Myotis, Spotted Bat, and Townsend's Western Big-eared Bat
Fringed myotis (Myotis thysanodes) use a wide variety of habitat types, including forest edges and
openings (Keinath 2004). Spotted bats (Euderma maculatum) are found throughout the west in a variety
of habitat types, often near cliffs and permanent water (Priday and Luce 1999). Townsend's western bigeared bats (Corynorhinus townsendii) roost in buildings, mines, and caves, and often forage along forest
margins (Kunz and Martin 1982).
Remote ultrasonic surveys were completed to identify foraging bats within the project area. Two
ultrasonic bat detectors were deployed in the mining boundary in July of 2021. None of these species
were detected. These three bat species are rare in northern Utah and a lack of detections at the site was
expected.
It is possible that these species occasionally utilize the parcels as foraging habitat, and the Silver Mine
would impact foraging and roosting habitat, and possibly winter hibernacula considering the extensive
rocky outcrops in the project area. However, given the lack of detections and wide foraging range of these
three species, impacts would likely be limited, and would not likely result in any population-level effects.
Wildlife Habitat
The project area overlaps approximately 629.1 acres of wildlife habitat, with a mix of forested, shrubland,
and grassland and forb habitat present throughout the various parcels. Approximately 64.7 acres of
forested habitat, 355.4 acres of shrubland habitat, 104.5 acres of rocky outcrop, and 104.5 acres of
grassland and forb habitat are present in the project area. The loss of habitat would represent a small
fraction of available habitat in the surrounding area. Due to the large size of the project and the fairly
narrow width of Parleys Canyon, habitat connectivity would be somewhat disrupted for terrestrial species
moving east or west, up or down the canyon. The movement path along the ridgetop would remain
unimpacted. The species most likely to be impacted by movement restriction caused by the presence of
the Silver Mine would be large home range big game species such as those discussed below. The level of
impact this movement barrier might have been difficult to determine without further data or study. Data
from large-scale telemetry studies conducted by the Utah Division of Wildlife Resources may provide
insight into this question.
Big Game
Five species of big game have overlapping ranges within the project area, black bear (Ursus americanus),
elk (Cervus canadensis), moose (Aloes aloes), mule deer (Odocoileus hemionus), and mountain lion
(Puma concolor) (Table 109.2-1).
