Black Bear
Black bear utilize a wide variety of habitat types but are typically associated with forested habitat for
foraging and denning. The Silver Mine project area is predominantly shrubs and grassland, with forested
habitat making up approximately 10 percent (64.7 acres) of the project area. Home ranges for black bear
are often large, and female black bear can have overlapping home ranges. In the western states, black bear
average approximately 0.8 bears per square mile, although a low-density population in northern Utah was
found to be closer to 0.03 bears per square mile (UDWR 2011).
The large home range, elusive nature and wide-ranging movement patterns of black bear make surveying
for this species difficult. Therefore, no surveys were completed for this species. Bear scat was found near
a moose carcass in the project area indicating that the area is utilized by black bears. Due to the low
population density and large home ranges, impacts on black bears would be limited, and would not result
in any population-level effects.
Elk
Elk utilize higher elevation areas in the summer, typically in aspen conifer forests, and then move down
to mid and lower elevation areas in the winter. The Silver Mine project area is predominantly shrubs and
grassland, with forested habitat making up approximately 10 percent (64.7 acres) of the project area.
Approximately 612 acres of the project area overlaps with designated elk crucial winter habitat (UDWR
2021c). This overlap accounts for approximately 6 percent of the continuous designated crucial winter
habitat that surrounds the project area. Additionally, approximately 7 acres of substantial spring and fall
habitat overlaps with the southeastern corner of the project area (UDWR 2021c). This overlap accounts
for less than 1 percent of the continuous designated substantial spring and fall habitat that surrounds the
project area (Figure 12).
Anecdotal pellet surveys during April, May, and June showed substantial elk use in the project area.
Given the identification of pellets and designated winter habitat within the project area, habitat impacts
would be expected. Due to the size of the project and overlap with crucial winter habitat, effects from
habitat loss and/or habitat fragmentation could be measurable at the population level. The level of impact
this project might have is difficult to determine without further data or large-scale study. Data from largescale telemetry studies conducted by the Utah Division of Wildlife Resources may provide insight into
this question.

Moose
Suitable habitat for moose includes areas with shrubs and young trees for browsing, which often includes
river bottoms and riparian habitat. However, moose do not require these features, and can be found
ranging far from any obvious water source so long as browse is available. Moose often use dense conifer
forests for thermoregulation and shelter in the winter and the summer. The Silver Mine project area is
predominantly shrubs and grassland, with forested habitat making up approximately 10 percent (64.7
acres) of the project area. The entirety of the project area overlaps with designated substantial winter
habitat (UDWR 2021c). This overlap accounts for approximately 2.5 percent of the continuous designated
substantial winter habitat the surrounds the project area (Figure 13).
Anecdotal pellet surveys during April, May, and June showed use by moose in the upper elevation
portions of the project area. Additionally, a moose carcass was located in the project area.
Given the identification of pellets, a carcass, and designated substantial winter habitat within the project
area, habitat impacts would be expected. The level of impact this project might have is difficult to
determine without further data or large-scale study. Data from large-scale telemetry studies conducted by
the Utah Division of Wildlife Resources may provide insight into this question.
Mule Deer
Suitable habitat for mule deer includes a wide range of habitat types, but generally includes shrubs and
open areas used for foraging near forested habitat for cover and thermoregulation. The Silver Mine
project area is predominantly shrubs and grassland, with forested habitat making up approximately 10
percent (64.7 acres) of the project area. Similar to elk, mule deer also move from higher elevation areas in
the summer to mid and lower elevations in the winter. In Utah, most mule deer are migratory, travelling
up to 70 miles between their winter and summer ranges (UDWR 2019). Approximately 590 acres of the
project area overlaps with crucial spring and fall habitat for mule deer (UDWR 2021c). This overlap
accounts for less than 1 percent of the continuous crucial spring and fall habitat that surrounds the project
area (Figure 14).
Anecdotal pellet surveys during April, May, and June showed use by mule deer in all portions of the
project area.
Given the identification of pellets and designated spring and fall habitat within the project area, impacts
on suitable habitat would be expected. The level of impact this project might have is difficult to determine
without further data or large-scale study. Data from large-scale telemetry studies conducted by the Utah
Division of Wildlife Resources may provide insight into this question.
Mountain Lion
Mountain lion occupy a wide variety of habitat types, ranging from mountainous forested habitat to lower
elevation open habitat. Their choice of habitat tends to be driven by the type of prey they are targeting.
Home ranges of mountain lion can be very large, over 190 square miles in some areas (Tesky 1995).
The large home range, elusive nature and wide-ranging movement patterns of mountain lion make
surveying for this species difficult. Therefore, no surveys were completed for this species. It is assumed
that this species could utilize the project area. Given the small size of the project area compared to a
single mountain lion home range, impacts to mountain lions would be limited and would not result in any
population-level effects.
Riparian Areas
Habitat surveys were completed to identify riparian habitat in any of the parcels. No riparian areas are
present.

Waterfowl and Riparian Areas
Habitat surveys were completed to identify aquatic habitat in any of the parcels that might provide habitat

for waterfowl. Waterfowl, with a few exceptions, prefer standing or slow-moving water for foraging and
resting. Some waterfowl species foraging exclusively in aquatic habitat, while others utilize water for
resting and forage mostly in upland habitat.
No riparian areas or aquatic habitat were identified in the project area. Given the lack of habitat, no
impacts are expected.
Minimization or Mitigation Measures for Wildlife Impacts
As discussed above, the impacts of the Silver Mine on most wildlife species are negligible. For species
with such impacts, no minimization or mitigation measures would be needed. For elk, moose, and mule
deer impacts due primarily to potential movement disruption could require mitigation if data from the
Utah Division of Wildlife Resources shows that significant movement corridors exist in the project area.
A variety of measures could apply, and the scale of these measures would be dependent upon the scale of
the impacts.
109.3 - Impacts on existing soils resources
Impacts to existing soil and plants by the proposed mining operations will consist of removing the soil
and plant material from the underlying rock material. This soil has been and will be used as vegetated
berms during mine operations. Once completed, stockpiled topsoil will be used during revegetation of the
site upon completion. The existing vegetation will be destroyed during this scrubbing and storing process.
There will be no impacts to riparian habitat and/or wetland areas from the proposed mining operations.
These habitat types are not present in the proposed permit area.
109.4 - Slope stability, erosion control, air quality, safety
Slope Stabilio
Tree Farm does not intend to negatively impact the surrounding environment. The facility will be mined
in a manner consistent with the regulations set by DOGM and leave a stable slope of 1.0 H:1.0 V,
including benching. A minimum factor of safety of 1.25 or greater will be maintained at all times on slope
stability. If there are any unforeseen changes in geologic, geomechanical properties or phreatic surface,
the slope stability will be reevaluated. The overall toe to crest slope will not exceed 1.0 H:1.0 V.
Erosion Control
Operation will be performed to control water and erosion in disturbed, bonded areas. The quarry floor will
be mined with a 1% grade sloping back into the pit face. The negative slope will cause all stormwater
entering the quarry to remain there until evaporated or absorbed into the ground. Until the quarry face is
complete, refer to the SWPPP, Appendix E. Any water that does slope away from the mine face (towards
the north) will be collected in a vegetated stormwater detention pond before discharging off site (Figure
3).
Erosion from dirt and dust from the roads will be controlled through the application of chemical treatment
such as magnesium or calcium chloride, graveling of the road, and grading it to have sufficient crown and
drainage ditches to the side so that the water does not pond. Sufficient turn-outs from road ditches will be
provided to allow water collecting on the road to be released in a non-erosive manner. Erosion of
stockpiles is addressed under soils above.
Air Quality
During operations at the Silver Mine Pit, Tree Farm will ensure that all regulations regarding air quality,
water quality, and oil storage will be strictly adhered to. The UDAQ has a set of regulations requiring
dust suppression and maximum allowable opacity generated from the operations and the site. Tree Farm
or a Tree Farm subcontractor will obtain a temporary relocation permit for initial operations (first year)

and a subsequent air quality approval order (AO) to commence operations at this location. To obtain the
AO, the operator will evaluate the impacts imposed by the facility to ensure no detrimental impact to the
surrounding public health and air quality will occur and ensure that operations would meet National
Ambient Air Quality Standards (NAAQS). NAAQS are developed for criteria pollutants and ensure the
health and safety of the public.
Water Quality
Tree Farm will obtain an UDWQ Utah Pollutant Discharge Elimination System (UPDES) Multi-Sector
General Permit for Storm Water Discharges associated with Industrial Activity permit for the Silver Mine
Pit. Compliance with this permit will ensure there will be no negative impact from the project area, as
required for permit coverage, Tree Farm will develop a comprehensive SWPPP that will be continually
updated to match changes at the facility for continued compliance with NPDES regulations.
As required by federal rule 40 CFR 112, Spill Prevention, Control, and Countermeasure (SPCC)
Regulation, the site will create and maintain a site specific SPCC plan. The plan will guide the facility on
maintaining any oil containing equipment or tanks to prevent spills.
Safety
Tree Farm adheres to both MSHA and Occupational Safety and Health Act (OSHA) guidelines. If there
is a conflict between the two regulations, the more stringent will be chosen.
Tree Farm also proposes to construct berms and fences along the pertinent section of the mine site,
including along property boundaries where reasonable access by a trespasser could be achieved. Tree
Farm will place warning signs along the perimeter to deter illegal access. Where possible trees will be
preserved along the northern portion of the site to limit visible impacts of the site along I-80.
Tree Farm will access the site via the Mount Aire exit and the UDOT frontage road along the south side
of I-80. The off/on ramps and UDOT frontage road provide access to the site from both the east and west
bound lanes of I-80 without adversely impacting the traffic and safety in the area. The road access is
illustrated further in Figure 3.
Fugitive Dust
Tree Farm will have a Fugitive Dust Control Plan, which will depict the necessary controls that will be
implemented to control fugitive dust emission and meet state regulations for the proper maintenance and
management of all fugitive dust standards._ Primary controls that are consistent with industry operations and
that will be implemented for this operation include the application of water to establish and or maintain
stabilization of surface soils, to maintain compliance with opacity standards and permit conditions. Water
used for dust suppression and compaction will derive from a water rights well site. Fugitive dust will be
controlled by applying water to vehicle traffic areas in the staging areas and unpaved routes, reducing
vehicle speed, and watering stockpiles to form crusts. Dust control will take place during working hours,
when leaving the site there will be a clean trackout to entering the paved surfaces.
The fugitive dust control permit holds the operations of this site accountable to opacity standards that shall
not exceed 10% at the property boundaries and 20% on site. Trained and certified personnel will conduct
Method 9 Opacity tests as needed to ensure that opacity is within compliant ranges, in addition to the
implementation of preventive and continuous active dust control applications.
Activities, including blasting soil & rock, clearing for site preparation, crushing and material processing,
hauling materials, screening of rock & soils, managing stock piles, and all others are detailed within the
Fugitive Dust Control Plan, as to how and what controls will be applied. Every effort to minimize fugitive
dust will be applied where applicable, including actionable controls and managing protocol, such as
minimizing and or limiting vehicle speed, total miles traveled, amounts of traffic, storage location of stock

piles, and many others.
It should be noted that weather, specifically wind plays a major role in fugitive dust management. Opacity
in R307-309-5(1) shall not apply when the wind speed exceeds 25 miles per hour if the owner or operator
has implemented, and continues to implement, the accepted fugitive dust control plan in R307-309-6 and
administers at least one of the following contingency measures, including pre-event watering or hourly
watering, one or both of which this operation has selected to perform as needed.
109.5 - Actions to mitigate any impacts
All actions taken to mitigate the impact to the surrounding environment are described in the preceding
sections and are summarized below
1. Air Quality
a. Water sprays on:
i. Conveyor drop points
ii. Crushers and screen
b. Road dust controls include
i. Paving and sweeping roads
ii. Water unpaved roads
iii. Chemical dust suppression
c. Track-Out
i. Continual sweeping
ii. Tire wash (if needed)
iii. Paving haul road from northside of facility to the Mount Aire on ramps.
2. Water Quality
a. Stormwater treatment. All stormwater will be treated on site to prevent sediment run off
above state industrial stormwater limits.
b. Seeding and vegetation of berms and other nonutilized/exposed areas to prevent soil
erosion
c. Track-Out controls
d. House Keeping
i. Properly disposing of trash and other solid waste
ii. Toilets properly maintained
e. Spill Prevention
i. SPCC plan updated and modified as needed
ii. All >55-gallon containers with an oil containing substance will be placed within
secondary containment
iii. Regular inspections of containers, spill prevention, and spill response materials
f. Groundwater Protection
i. Maintain a 10 ft buffer from the lowest point of the mine floor (including
stormwater retention pond) above groundwater
ii. Line all stormwater retention ponds with clay or a similar low permeability
material
3. Nuisance Issues
a. Noise and Light
i. Use of white noise backup alarms on loading equipment
ii. Limiting operation hours to 6 AM to 10 PM when possible
iii. Directing lights in a downward direction and away from the hill sides and 1-80

R647-4-110 - Reclamation Plan
Tree Farm will comply with all rules set forth in R647-4-110, rules in the Mined Land Reclamation Act
and any associated administrative rules.
110.1 - Current & post mining land use
Previous use has included precious mineral mining, hard rock quarry and cement powder production.

After operations at the Silver Mine project area are complete the land will be restored to the anticipate
post mining land use. The proposed postmining land use will be at the discretion of the property owner
and could potentially be recreational development such as lodging, trail construction for biking, hiking,
and cross country skiing. Access trails to National Forest areas south and west of the proposed mine site
may also be established.
110.2 - Roads, highwalls, slopes, drainages, pits, etc., reclaimed
Reclamation of roads
The access road leading to the northern edge of the mine site will not be reclaimed and will be left in
place to provide access for post mining uses. All other temporary access roads within the mine plan will
be reclaimed and contoured to match the slopes illustrated in Figures 4 and 5.
The mine is anticipated to be worked in stages from the north end leading south. Once the material is
mined near the northern property boundary to establish a pad for the crushing and screening equipment,
the excavation of material will continue to the south with the gradual development of benches forming the
slope illustrated in Figures 4 and 5.
The portion of any access road needed to work a bench will be reclaimed as the next bench in the
sequence is developed. Once reclamation is completed down to the lower benches, any bench access
roads above will have been reclaimed other than the facility access road described above and illustrated in
Figure 3. Reclamation of the temporary access roads includes grading the road location to blend with
surrounding topography, distributing and scarifying topsoil to leave a roughened surface and then seeded
as described in Section 109.2.
Reclamation of highwalls and slopes
The exposed slope resulting from mining activities will be sequentially terraced while mining is in
progress to provide an overall slope of 1.0H:1.0V from the toe to the crest as shown in Figure 5. Typical
benches will be 50 feet high and 26 feet across, with the intermediate slopes at 0.5 H:1.0 V. Safety berms
5 feet wide and 2 feet high will be constructed along the outer edge of each bench using waste rock
material. Scavenged topsoil and amended waste fines will be distributed on the benches and berms and
seeded with a mix as described below in Section 110.5.
Reclamation of impoundments. pits and ponds
Impoundments or ponds used to retain stormwater on site during mining activities will be reclaimed by
backfilling the excavations or removing the berms to prevent impoundment of stormwater after mining.
The site will be graded to drain to the north to match preexisting conditions.
The ephemeral drainages that cross the site (illustrated in Figure 6) will be impacted by mining activities.
As the mine progresses towards depletion, channels will be created to divert the ephemeral streams away
from operational equipment and towards the original discharge point on the north side of the mine
disturbance. A pond and channel design and hydrologic analysis are included in Appendix D.

The stormwater impoundment that will be used for stormwater treatment before discharge on the north
end of the site will be left in place and graded to encourage a wetland habitat. The reconstructed
ephemeral streams will discharge into the pond before exiting the site at or near the original stream
locations.
Waste Dumps
No waste dumps will be constructed.
Shafts and Adits
None of these items are associated with this project.
Drill Holes
If any drill holes are required, they will be plugged and sealed as described in R647-4-108 above. There
will be no drill holes left upon reclamation.
Tailings
There are no tailings associated with this project.
Leach Pads
There are no leach pads associated with this project.
Stockpiles
No stockpile will remain at the time of final reclamation.
Equipment, buildings and other structures
There are no permanent buildings or structures proposed for the site. All structures and equipment will be
removed from the site to be used elsewhere or disposed of offsite, at an approved landfill, upon
completion of mining activities and before reclamation is complete.
110.3 - Description of facilities to be left (post mining use)
All surface facilities on site will be removed at the termination of mining. No facilities or structures will
remain on site.
110.4 - Description or treatment/disposition of deleterious or acid forming material
There are no known naturally-occurring deleterious materials on the site. This is a limestone-based gravel
mining operation that will not be liberating any toxic or acid-forming materials requiring neutralization.
All man-made structures, trash and other waste will be removed and disposed of at an approved local
sanitary landfill or other appropriate facility.
110.5 - Revegetation planting program
The following procedures have been designed to restore the vegetation community in the project area to
natural, pre-disturbance conditions as much as possible. As described previously, the floor of the mine
and bench surfaces are assumed to be reclaimed and would occur as described in the sections below.
Soil Material Replacement
The total acreage of the mine floor and benches would be 143.5 and 240 acres, respectively. To provide
adequate planting material and ground cover, stockpiled topsoil berms would be re-distributed using large
equipment to a minimum depth of 6 inches over the disturbed areas on the mine floor and benches. The
topsoil material would come from stockpiled topsoil removed from the site, amended waste fines, or
hauled in from other sources, if necessary. The depth of topsoil on the site varies widely with steeper

slopes having shallower soil cover. Based on final topography of the graded mine plan and plant
requirements, approximately 331,540 cubic yards of soil would be required during reclamation of the
Silver Mine disturbed area.
The soil material used for reclamation should match the properties and qualities of the NRCS descriptions
for soil map units in the project area prior to disturbance, as much as practicable. Soil properties measured
from each soil unit are found in Table 106.5-3. Laboratory test results for pH, salinity, and sodium
adsorption ratio (SAR) indicate that soils meet the highest category of soil suitability (good) used to
evaluate reclamation potential (DOGM 2000). Measurements of soil texture (Table 106.5-2) and
estimates of available water holding capacity based on texture (USU Extension 2001) were also evaluated
for reclamation potential. Except for Site #4 (Unit 151) all results are classified in the good category for
reclamation potential. The soil sample in Unit 151 had a clay texture which is in the Fair and Poor
categories for reclamation potential (DOGM 2000). However, soil survey results indicate that Unit 151
has a gravelly loam texture.
Soil nutrients have an important influence on plant viability and revegetation success. Irrigated agriculture
and other cultivated areas use fertilizer to help seeds establish and improve crop production. However,
fertilizer use on arid rangelands is not recommended (USU Extension 2001). Fertilizer is beneficial only
to rangelands during years with high precipitation. During dry years, fertilizer applications on rangeland
can create problems with forage yields and plant vigor (NRCS 2000, AFRD 2003). Soil nutrients in the
Silver Mine area are lower than levels typically recommended for agricultural fields. However, based on
the extent and density of native species, nutrient levels are sufficient to sustain healthy vegetation
communities.
Seed Bed Preparation
The soil material would be prepared into a seed bed to facilitate seed establishment. The soil material on
the benches and the floor of the mine would be either ripped or disked to a minimum of 6 inches and the
surface would be left in a rough condition to enhance water harvesting, erosion control, and revegetation
success. Compacted surfaces across the area would be ripped to a minimum of 12 inches prior to applying
the topsoil.
Seed Mixture
Mining activities on the Silver Mine parcels will occur in five plant communities as described in Section
106.7 and listed in Table 106.7-3.
All species on the planting list (Table 110.5-1 are perennial plants selected for their compatibility with the
growing conditions (e.g., drought and temperature tolerance, shading, exposure) and soil types, present in
the project area, and/or successes on similar reclamation projects. Most are native species, and all are
species commonly used in reclamation and restoration projects in similar settings. These species would
provide basic soil and watershed protection and support post-mining land use.
Once the seed bed has been prepared, a seed mix of grass, forb, and shrubs would be drilled or broadcast
over the project area. The seed mix includes cultivars or varieties with a proven record of successful
establishment on disturbed sites with similar precipitation as the project area (USDA 2020b), and which
would be compatible with a wide range of post-mining land uses (Table 110.5-1). Seeds would be applied
at a total rate of approximately 17.2 pounds per acre of pure live seed (PLS).
To meet the rules established in the Utah Administrative Code, revegetation of the bench surfaces would
be considered complete when vegetation cover is equal to at least 70 percent of the pre-mining cover (i.e.,
36 percent), and when the vegetation has survived three growing seasons following the last seeding.

Seeding Method
Seed would be either aerially broadcast or drilled in using a rangeland drill. If a rangeland drill is used,
the appropriate drilling depths range from 0.1 to 1 inch, as shown on the USDA planting guides in
Appendix B, though a depth of 0.5 inches would be appropriate for grass species and a depth of 0.25
inches would be appropriate for the forb species in the seed mix. The seeding of grass and forb species
would take place in the late fall to increase seed establishment.
If noxious weeds become established after the reclamation seeding and planting, they will be treated in
accordance with the Utah Noxious Weed Law to minimize spread across the project area or onto adjacent
properties.
Fertilization and Soil Amendments
Composted manure could be incorporated into the topsoil after it has been redistributed on the site, if

needed, based on soil chemistry analysis. Even though soil test results from nearly all samples are in the
good category for reclamation potential (Table 106.5-3), the soils will still be amended to improve
vegetation growth. If topsoil from other locations or wash fines are used, analysis of the soil will
determine if and what type of fertilizer or amendments would be required. The plant species selected for
revegetation are relatively tolerant of nutrient-poor conditions.
Other Revegetation Procedures
Depending on analysis of soil once the reclamation area has been prepared, mulch may be used to
increase the success of the planting. Mulch may also be used on sites with steeper slopes.
Specific Reclamation Action Areas
Berms
The topsoil from the mine area would be temporarily stored in berms on the perimeter of the project area.
The remainder of the topsoil required would be hauled in from offsite. After the topsoil has been
redistributed to the benches, slopes, and mine bottom, the area beneath the berm would be revegetated
using the seed mix and planting rates listed in Table 110.5-1
Benches and Slopes
Revegetation of the benches and slopes would commence as soon as possible after mine development has
been completed, or sooner if possible. During the final revegetation, topsoil would be redistributed from
the topsoil stored in the perimeter berms or hauled in from off-site onto the benches and slopes to an
approximate depth of 6 inches, due to the expanded area requiring surface cover of the topsoil. The
benches and slopes would then be revegetated using the seed mix and planting rates in Table 110.5-1.
Mine Bottom
Revegetation of the mine bottom would proceed similar to the process described for the benches. After
mining operations are complete and the final topography has been achieved, the area would be ripped or
disked and stockpiled topsoil would be redistributed from the berms created during Phase 1 to an
approximate depth of 6 inches. The bottom of the mine would then be revegetated using the seed mix and
planting rates listed in Table 110.5-1.
R647-4-112 — Variance
Tree Farm is not requesting a variance to the Division of Oil, Gas, and Mining's regulations. If a variance
is needed in the future, Tree Farm will request that variance as an amendment or revision to this Notice of
Intent.
R647-4-113 — Surety
Tree Farm has determined the following surety in the amount of $3,144,000 to cover DOGM's cost in the
event of bond forfeiture for the entire 634 acres. The calculations are included in Appendix F. The bond
amount was calculated based on data provided by the Department, the Caterpillar Handbook, R.S. Means,
Equipment Watch's Blue Book Values, and Rental Rates provided by Caterpillar. The bond amount was
calculated assuming, worst case scenario, the entire site would be reclaimed at once at the end of the mine
life.

Parleys Canyon Silver Mine — Resource Inventory and Reclamation Plan

TREE FARM, LLC:

RECLAMATION PLAN

CIRRUS ECOLOGICAL SOLUTIONS, LC

NOVEMBER 2021

Parleys Canyon Silver Mine — Resource Inventory and Reclamation Plan

INTRODUCTION
Tree Farm, LLC requested that Cirrus Ecological Solutions (Cirrus) assist with the preparation of an
operation plan, specifically as it relates to mapping, soils, vegetation, and wildlife (including endangered
species) as well as a reclamation plan for the Silver Mine area located in Parleys Canyon. Tree Farm, LLC
intends to develop portions of the property as a rock and gravel mine. This report is in support of submitting
a notice of intention to commence large mining operations to the Division of Oil, Gas, & Mining (DOGM).
The project area is located on privately-owned land in Township 1 South and Range 2 East, Sections 16,
17, 20, and 21 Salt Lake Base and Meridian (Figure 1). The project area is approximately 638 acres in size
and is located in an unincorporated area on the south side of Interstate 80 in Parleys Canyon east of Salt
Lake City, Utah. Generally, it is surrounded by National Forest System lands on the south and west and
private land on the north. Access to the property is from Interstate 80 and limited unimproved roads. The
elevation ranges from approximately 5,250 feet to 7,560 feet above mean sea level (Figure 2).
PROJECT SUMMARY
Parcels, general locations, and acreages associated with the Silver Mine large mine area are shown in Table
1. All properties associated with the proposed mine are owned by Tree Farm, LLC. The total number of
acres to be mined is approximately 617.
Table 1. Parcel numbers, general location, parcel acres, and mining acres associated with the Silver
Mine.

Tree Farm, LLC proposes to develop the project area (Figure 3) in phases. During the first phase, topsoil
(this report defines topsoil as surface cover material consisting of darker soils containing organic matter)
would be stored on site in berms along the perimeter of the mining boundary where possible for
redistribution following mining. The remainder of the topsoil would be mined with the rest of the material.
During the second phase, the mine would be excavated as shown in Figure 3. The walls of the mine would
be terraced with an overall 0.5:1 (horizontal:vertical) slope ratio and would consist of a series of 50-foottall slopes separated by a 26-foot-wide bench (Figure 4).
The third phase would consist of post-mining reclamation. A major part of that reclamation work would be
revegetation of the benches and floor of the mine. The proposed mine plan illustrates the deepest extent of
mining at the site will be at the elevation of approximately 5,300 feet (Figure 3). The reclamation plan
developed in this report assumes the mine floor will be revegetated. Following mining completion,
reclamation activities will consist of topsoil redistribution from around the perimeter. The replaced topsoil
would likely require amendments. Prior to revegetation, the first step would be the re-application of stored
topsoil (including amendments) onto these areas to provide a medium for plant establishment. The
subsequent step would include planting appropriate shrub, grass, and forb seeds on the benches. The plants
Cirrus Ecological Solutions, LC

1

Parleys Canyon Silver Mine — Resource Inventory and Reclamation Plan
recommended in this revegetation plan are species similar to those which are currently present on the site
or are typical for the respective soil units.

EXISTING CONDITIONS
A review of aerial imagery taken between 1993 and 2020 show that limited powerline and road development
has occurred on some parcels within the project area during that time. Overall, the vegetation communities
throughout the project area do not appear to have changed significantly over that time period.
Cirrus' wildlife biologist, botanist, and watershed scientist surveyed the project area in the spring/summer
of 2021. During these site visits, wildlife surveys were completed, the existing vegetation and relevant
characteristics of the overall setting were recorded, and soil test pits were sampled.
As appropriate, existing conditions are further described in the resource sections below.

REPORT STRUCTURE
In accordance with the scope of work, this report has been divided into appropriate resource sections. These
resource sections include:
105.1 - Base map
106.5 - Existing soil types, location of plant growth material
106.7 — Existing vegetative communities to establish revegetation success
109.2 — Wildlife habitat and endangered species
110.5 — Revegetation planting program and topsoil redistribution
Each resource section begins with requirements stated in the DOGM application shown in italics.

105.1 - BASE MAP
A topographic base map (or maps) with appropriate contour intervals must be submitted with this notice.
... The following information shall be included:
2. Perennial streams, springs, and other bodies of water; ... within 500feet of the proposed
mining operations.
All mapped water features were identified on 1:24,000 scale USGS maps (Figure 2) including any streams,
springs, or ponds in the project area.
The length of mapped water features in the Silver Mine disturbed area and 500 ft. of the proposed mining
operation includes (Figure 5):
•
•
•

Canals/ditches = 973 ft.
Perennial streams (Parleys Creek) = 1,688 ft.
Ephemeral streams = 17,663 ft.

A reconnaissance-level field survey was completed to verify the location of mapped water features and
identify any unmapped surface water. Except for Parleys Creek drain, no water was identified in stream
channels. Stream channel features were identified at some locations, but no indication of surface water was
observed. Ponds, springs, seeps, and other forms of surface or ground water discharge were not observed
during the site survey in the proposed mining area or 500 ft. buffer.
Underground municipal water is transferred by pipeline between Mountain Dell Reservoir and Salt Lake
City's water service area. Parleys Creek enters an underground channel 0.25 miles downstream of Mountain
Dell Reservoir and flows beneath the interstate for approximately four miles to the mouth of Parleys
Canyon. Some stormwater runoff collects in ephemeral tributaries to Parley's Creek. Any water in these
tributaries is diverted into stormwater drains located near the highway passing through Parleys Canyon.

106.5 - EXISTING SOIL TYPES, LOCATION OF PLANT GROWTH MATERIAL
Specific information on existing soils to be disturbed by mining will be required. General soils information
may not be sufficient.
Provide specific descriptions of the existing soil resourcesfound in the area. Soil types should be identified
along with depth and extent, especially those to be directly impacted by mining.
The plan shall include an Order 3 Soil Survey (or similar) and map. This information is needed to determine
which soils are suitable for stockpiling for revegetation. This soil data may be available from the local
Natural Resources Conservation Service office, or if on public lands, from the land management agency.
The map needs to be of such scale that soil types can be accurately determined on the ground.
(a)

Each soil type to be disturbed needs to be field analyzedfor the following:
Depth of soil material
Volume (for stockpiling)
Texture ffield determination
pH (field determination)

(b)

Where there are problem soil areas (as determinedfrom the field examination) laboratory
analysis may be necessary. Soil samples to be sent to the laboratoryfor analysis need to be
about one quart in size, properly labeled, and in plastic bags. Each of the soil horizons on
some sites may need to be sampled. Soil sample locations need to be shown on the soils map.
The following parameters are normally needed.• texture, pH, Ec (conductivity), CEC (Cation
Exchange Capacity), SAR, % Organic Matter, Total N, Available Phosphorus (as P205),
Potassium (as K20), and acid/base potential.

Information on existing soil resources in the project area were acquired through the USDA Natural
Resource Conservation Service (NRCS) Web Soil Survey (WSS). The data shows nine soil units are in the
project area (Figure 6). This information describes existing soil conditions with sufficient detail to meet the
requirements for an Order 3 Soil Survey (Table 2). One soil sample was collected from each soil unit at
representative locations (Figure 6). Measures of pH and estimates of soil texture were also determined for
each soil sample (Table 3).
Soil pits were excavated using a pick and shovel down to the lower boundary of the B horizon at each
sample location. Tools were cleaned between each sample site to prevent sample contamination. The overall
depth of each soil pit varied based on local conditions of the C horizon but were generally 20-36 inches
deep. A composite sample was gathered from the A and B horizons by collecting subsamples at roughly 4inch intervals along the entire profile. Composite samples were stored in sterile plastic bags and transported
to the laboratory for testing within 24 hours of sample collection.
A brief summary of properties for each soil unit in the proposed mine area is included below. Additional
detail is found in Appendix A.
101 Agassiz-Rock outcrop complex, 30 to 70 percent slopes
Agassiz and similar soils make up 60 percent of the composition in this soil unit. Rock outcrop comprises
25 percent, and other minor components cover 15 percent of this unit. Soil depth is shallow, and a typical
profile has a depth to lithic bedrock of 14 inches. Soil texture is classified as very cobbly loam. Field
observations from Site 2 indicate sandy clay loam (Table 3). Soils are derived from limestone and
excessively drained. A total of 199.3 acres of the proposed mine area is mapped as soil unit 101.

136 Hades-Agassiz-Rock outcrop complex, 30 to 70 percent slimes
Hades and similar soils comprise 50 percent of this soil unit. Agassiz and Rock outcrop comprise 30 and
10 percent respectively, and other minor components cover 10 percent of this unit. Soil depth is typically
60 inches having an A and B horizon with a restrictive layer at a depth of 80 inches. The A horizon extends
to 18 inches below the surface, and the B horizon is present to a depth of 60 inches. The texture is loam
through the A horizon and clay loam in the B horizon. Field observations at Site 1 indicate soil texture is
primarily loam. Soils are derived from sandstone, quartzite, and shale and well drained. A total of 155.6
acres of the proposed mine area is mapped as soil unit 136.

151 Lucky Star gravelly loam, 30 to 60 percent slopes
Lucky Star and similar soils comprise 85 percent of the soil unit, with other minor components covering 15
percent. Soil depth is typically 80 inches including an A, E, and B horizon. The A horizon extends to a
depth of 12 inches below the surface followed by an E horizon to a depth of 25 inches, and a B horizon
extending to 80 inches. Soil texture includes gravelly loam in the A horizon, very gravelly fine sandy loam
in the E horizon, and very cobbly sandy loam to very cobbly sandy clay loam in the B horizon. Field
observations of texture in Site 4 samples identified a predominately clay texture. Soils are derived from
conglomerate and sandstone and well drained. A total of 25.0 acres of the proposed mine area is mapped
as soil unit 151.
BCC; Brad very rocky loamy sand, 40 to 80 percent slope
Brad and similar soils comprise 60 percent of the soil unit. Rock outcrop comprises 25 percent and other
minor components cover 15 percent of this unit. Depth to bedrock ranges from 14 to 24 inches throughout
the unit. The A horizon typically extends to 8 inches below the surface. No B horizon is described for this
soil unit. Soil texture in the A horizon is typically very cobbly loamy sand. The soil sample collected from
Site 3 was observed to have a loam texture with a high percentage of sand. Soils are derived from sandstone
and are excessively drained. A total of 25.0 acres is mapped in the proposed mine area as soil unit BCG.
BEG Bradshaw-Agassiz association, steep
Bradshaw and similar soils comprise 55 percent of the soil unit. The remainder of the unit includes Agassiz
(35 percent) and minor components (10 percent). Depth to bedrock is over 80 inches. The typical depth of
the A horizon extends to 20 inches below the surface followed by the B horizon down to a depth of 52
inches. Soil texture is very silty loam throughout this depth. Soil texture from Site 7 was observed to be silt
loam. Soils are derived from limestone, sandstone, and shale and well drained. A total of 114.6 acres is
mapped in the proposed mine area as soil unit BEG.
DCG Deer Creek loam, 30 to 60 percent slopes
Deer Creek and similar soil comprise 90 percent of the soil unit and 10 percent minor components. The
depth to bedrock is over 80 inches. The A horizon extends to a depth of 11 inches, and the B horizon
continues to a depth of 45 inches. Soils on the surface are loam through the A horizon, transition to clay
loam down to a depth of 16 inches, and gravelly clay down to 45 inches. The observed texture at Site 6 was
Cirrus Ecological Solutions, LC

11

Parleys Canyon Silver Mine — Resource Inventory and Reclamation Plan
silt loam. Soils are derived from limestone, and well drained. A total of 34.1 acres is mapped in the proposed
mine area as soil unit DCG.
EMG Emigration very cobbly loam, 40 to TO percent slopes
Emigration and similar soil comprise 95 percent of this soil unit. The remaining 5 percent consists of minor
components. The typical depth to unweathered bedrock ranges from 18 to 28 inches. The A horizon is
shallow from the surface to four inches deep, and there is no B horizon. The texture is very cobbly loam
and confirmed from soil samples taken at Site 5. Soils are derived from weathered limestone and sandstone
and are considered well drained. A total of 69.1 acres is mapped in the proposed mine area as soil unit
EMG.
HHF Harkers Soils, 6 to 40 percent slopes
Harkers and similar soils comprise 90 percent of the soil unit, respectively. The remaining 10 percent
consists of minor components. The typical depth to bedrock is greater than 80 inches. The A horizon extends
to a depth of 14 inches, and the B horizon continues to a depth of 58 inches. Soil texture in the A horizon
is cobbly loam, and the B horizon consists of gravelly clay loam transitioning to gravelly clay with
increasing depth. Visual observations of soil samples at Site 8 identified primarily loam soil texture. Soils
are derived from limestone, sandstone, and shale and well drained. A total of 2.2 acres is mapped in the
proposed mine area as soil unit HHF.
RO Rock Land
Rock Land is considered a miscellaneous area in the soil survey with little to no soil associated with the
soil unit. Observations during the site visit confirmed the area is primarily rock scree consisting of cobble
to boulder-sized rock. Samples were taken at Site 9 to describe soil resource conditions. Soil texture at this
site was consistent with loam soil. A total of 10.0 acres in the proposed mine area is mapped as soil unit
RO.

106.7 - EXISTING VEGETATIVE COMMUNITIES TO ESTABLISH REVEGETATION
SUCCESS
Provide the Division with a description of the plant communities growing onsite and the percent vegetation
cover for each plant community. Describe the methodology used to obtain these values.
The percent ground cover is determined by sampling the vegetation type(s) on the areas to be mined. Use
standard vegetation cover measurement techniques, such as ocular estimation, line intercept, or the point
method, and describe the method used. The data needs to show percent cover by species, bare ground,
litter, and rock. Coverfrom these components should total 100 percent. The revegetation success standard
will be based on this information.
Descriptions of the plant communities in the project area were developed by surveying the different plant
communities. Aerial imagery was used to identify different plant communities in the project area.
Vegetation cover transects were established in each plant community. A total of nine, 100-meter long
transects were placed across all vegetation types. Cover was read at 1-meter increments. Aerial canopy
cover was recorded for each species intersected by the point. If multiple species of vegetation were
intersected by the point, as in overstory and understory layers, all species were recorded. Thus, total aerial
canopy cover may sum to greater than 100 percent. However, aerial canopy cover percentages shown in
Table 4 have been adjusted to show canopy cover with no overlap. Total aerial canopy cover in Table 4
includes overlapping vegetation layers for each individual plant species identified (Appendix B). In
addition, ground cover was also recorded. Categories of groundcover include bare ground, litter, rock, and
basal vegetation. Total ground cover sums to 100 percent (Table 4). Canopy cover and ground cover are
independent metrics.
Cirrus Ecological Solutions, LC

12

Parleys Canyon Silver Mine — Resource Inventory and Reclamation Plan

area. Table 4 summarizes the four plant communities that occur in the project area. These include deciduous

below. Table 5 lists the species that were identified during the field surveys across all transects. Appendix
B provides detailed transect summary data for each transect. Table 6 summarizes the acreage of each plant
community across the parcels which include the Silver Mine (Figure 7).

Deciduous Woodlands: Deciduous woodlands is the most common plant community occurring in the
project area (Figure 7). The cover and composition within the deciduous woodlands plant community
varies depending on slope, aspect, and elevation. Six deciduous woodlands subtypes were identified and
sampled (Table 4). Gambel oak is the dominant species. Co-dominant or subdominant tree/shrub species
include big-tooth maple, snowberry, chokecherry, and serviceberry. Oregon grape was also subdominant
in some locations. Herbaceous understory composition varied between subtypes. In some subtypes,
herbaceous understory was very limited. Other sites included a more prominent herbaceous understory
composed of such species as arrowleaf balsamroot, mule's ear, Wasatch desert parsley, and mountain
brome. Appendix B provides a list of species and corresponding cover for species comprising the deciduous
woodlands plant community.
Conifer Forest: Conifer forest occurs on north-east facing aspects. It is dominated by subalpine fir
overstory with a ninebark understory. Other important shrubs include chokecherry and serviceberry.
Herbaceous understory is limited. The most common species included heart-leaf arnica and elk sedge.
Litter provides high ground cover for this community type. Appendix B provides a list of species and
corresponding cover for species comprising the conifer forest community type.
Mountain Shrub-Rock Outcrop: Mountain shrub-rock outcrop occurs on steep, rocky slopes. It is shrubdominated. Gambel oak is the most common shrub, followed by rockspirea, buckwheat, curl-leaf,
mahogany, and sagebrush. Litter is limited in this community. Juniper occurs as scattered occurrences in
the community type as well. Appendix B provides a list of species and corresponding cover for species
comprising the mountain shrub-rock outcrop community type.
Grass Forb: The grass-forb community type occurs on northerly aspects. Dominate species include
bluebunch wheatgrass, mountain brome, and smooth brome, arrowleaf balsamroot, mules ear, and
buckwheat. Snowberry and ninebark are the dominant shrubs that occur in this community. Appendix B
provides a list of species and corresponding cover for species comprising the grass-forb community type.

109.2 - WILDLIFE HABITAT AND ENDANGERED SPECIES
List any threatened or endangered species or other species of special concern found in the area. Include a
negative declaration if these species or their habitats are not found. Describe the impacts on wildlife
habitat, big game, riparian areas, and waterfowl associated with this operation. Describe measures to be
taken to minimize or mitigate any impacts to wildlife or endangered species.
Threatened or Endangered Species
The project area falls within the potential range of two threatened or endangered species, the Canada lynx
(Lynx canadensis; threatened) and the western yellow-billed cuckoo (Coccyzus americanus; threatened).

Cirrus Ecological Solutions, LC

16

Parleys Canyon Silver Mine — Resource Inventory and Reclamation Plan
Canada Lynx
Canada lynx require high-elevation coniferous forests with a dense, complex understory for foraging and
denning. A typical home range for Canada lynx is large (often greater than 3,700 acres) and consists of a
matrix of high-quality foraging and denning habitat as well as lower-quality habitat that is used to move
among higher-quality areas (Ulev 2007). The Silver Mine project area is predominantly shrubs and
grassland, with forested habitat making up approximately 10 percent (64.7 acres) of the project area. Should
a Canada lynx home range overlap the project area, the area would likely provide movement habitat as
opposed to high-quality foraging or denning habitat, given its limited understory complexity and size.
The large home range, elusive nature, and wide-ranging movement patterns of Canada lynx make surveying
for this species difficult. Therefore, no surveys were completed for this species. Based on the poor quality
of the habitat present and the distance from known lynx populations, it is assumed that this species would
very rarely utilize the project area.
The Silver Mine would impact potential Canada lynx habitat. However, this habitat is not likely high-quality
foraging nor breeding habitat for Canada lynx that may be utilizing the area. Therefore, impacts on Canada
lynx would be limited to lower-quality matrix habitat, and would likely not result in any effects, and
certainly no population-level effects.
Western Yellow-billed Cuckoo
The Silver Mine project area does not provide habitat for western yellow-billed cuckoos. The project area
does not have riparian corridors with dense, continuous cottonwoods, willows, and shrubs required by
yellow-billed cuckoos for foraging and breeding (UDWR 2020). Furthermore, western yellow-billed
cuckoos are mainly known to occupy only two areas in Utah with consistency: north of the town of Green
River, and near the Ouray National Wildlife Refuge (UDWR 2020). The project area is not located between
these two core areas, and thus would not provide temporary movement habitat should individuals move
between the two areas.
Given the lack of habitat in the project areas, and distance from known populations, it is assumed this
species is not present. Therefore, no surveys were completed for this species. No impacts are expected.
Species of Special Concern
Species of special concern were selected from the Utah Sensitive Species list (UDWR 2017). While the
Utah Sensitive Species list is no longer maintained by the UDWR, it represents the best available list of
species of special concern in the area. Several migratory bird species were included that the U. S. Fish and
Wildlife Service has required analysis for on other projects in similar areas and habitats.
Prior to field surveys, aerial imagery was examined to identify broad habitat classes present in the project
area to eliminate any species from consideration that would not be present based on large-scale habitat
needs. Table 7 lists the species whose broad habitat needs were identified through aerial imagery. Initial
habitat surveys were then completed in the field to identify whether suitable habitat was available for each
species given their habitat needs within each broad habitat class.
Based on the results of the habitat surveys (Table 7), the only species of concern that could be impacted by
the Silver Mine project are northern goshawk, bald eagle, ferruginous hawk, short-eared owl, Lewis's
woodpecker, migratory birds, and the three species of bats.

Northern Goshawk
Suitable foraging habitat for northern goshawk (Accipiter gentiles) can be highly variable, with individuals
utilizing a wide range of habitat types. Breeding habitat for northern goshawk is more specific, with nests
often located in areas with dense canopy cover typical of older forests (USFS 2019). The Silver Mine
project area is predominantly shrubs and grassland, with forested habitat making up approximately 10
percent (64.7 acres) of the project area. Based on habitat models, the project area is located adjacent to high
quality northern goshawk habitat, which is defined as providing both high quality foraging and nesting
habitat (Graham et al. 1999). The project area itself appears to provide higher quality foraging habitat, but
lower quality breeding habitat (Graham et al. 1999).
Following the dawn acoustic protocol (USFS 2006), remote acoustic recorders were deployed near
potentially suitable forested habitat in the project area in April of 2021. Recorders were deployed twice in
the same locations, separated by two weeks to ensure adequate temporal coverage. The recordings were
subsequently analyzed for northern goshawk calls by viewing and listening to the spectrograms in Wildlife
Acoustics Kaleidoscope Pro software. No northern goshawks were detected at any of the recorders.
Given the diversity of habitat that northern goshawks use for foraging, and proximity to high quality nesting
habitat, it is possible that northern goshawks may use the project area for foraging. Therefore, some foraging
habitat may be impacted. Given the lack of detections within the forested habitat, lack of breeding habitat,
and larger home ranges of northern goshawks, impacts to northern goshawks would be limited, and would
not result in any population-level effects.
Bald Eagle
Bald eagles (Haliaeetus leucocephalus) build large stick nests in tall trees along waterways, and forage
great distances in search of food, including carrion. The project area does not provide any nesting habitat
or high-quality foraging habitat for bald eagles. However, bald eagles may occasionally utilize the project
area in search of carrion. Due to the likely sporadic use of the project area, no surveys were completed for
bald eagles. Bald eagles are assumed to occasionally be present in the project area. Given the likely limited
presence in the project area and lack of suitable breeding habitat, no impacts are expected.
Ferruginous Hawk
Suitable habitat for ferruginous hawks (Buteo regalis) includes dry, open habitat with scattered trees and
woodlands. They forage in open grassland and shrubland habitat where they can find abundant prey species,
Cirrus Ecological Solutions, LC

19

Parleys Canyon Silver Mine — Resource Inventory and Reclamation Plan
such as rabbits, ground squirrels and prairie dogs. Nesting habitat for ferruginous hawks can vary widely
based on habitat availability. If trees are available, ferruginous hawks will likely place their nest in a tree.
However, they also nest on the ground, along short cliff edges, or on top of powerline poles (Tesky 1994a).
Given the generalist nature of their foraging and nesting needs, surveys focused on identifying any stick
nests in the project area that may be used by ferruginous hawks. Remote aerial surveys were completed
with a UAV in June of 2021. These surveys were completed in a pattern that covered all suitable habitat in
the project area. No large stick nests were identified that might be attributed to ferruginous hawks. The
stick nests located on the cliffs in the northeastern-most parcel (Figure 8 below) could potentially have been
constructed by ferruginous hawks, but this species typically prefers trees when available.
This species may still utilize the project area for foraging, and the Silver Mine would impact foraging
habitat. Given the lack of nests identified in the area, and wide foraging range of this species, impacts to
ferruginous hawks would be limited, and would not result in any population-level effects.
Short-eared Owl
Suitable foraging habitat for short-eared owls (Asioflammeus) is typically open areas with short vegetation,
which is common throughout many parcels. Nests are built on the ground in tall grasses or shrubs (Wiggins
et al. 2020).
Point counts were conducted in suitable habitat in April and May of 2021 to identify any short-eared owls
that may be utilizing the area. Twelve point-counts were completed, distributed in habitats throughout the
project area. No short-eared owls were noted during the point count surveys.
It is possible short-eared owls may still utilize the area for foraging, and the Silver Mine would impact
foraging habitat. Given the lack of detections and wide availability of suitable habitat in the surrounding
area, impacts would be limited, and would not result in any population-level effects.
Lewis's Woodpecker
Suitable habitat for Lewis's woodpeckers (Melanerpes lewis) includes riparian woodlands, recently burned
forested habitat and ponderosa pine forests. Lewis's woodpeckers do not excavate for insects, as many other
woodpeckers do. Instead, they catch insects from the air during short flights. As such, they often occupy
areas with open canopy and low tree density to allow room for foraging. Nests are placed in trees that are
decayed and easy to excavate (Abele et al. 2004). The Silver Mine project area is predominantly shrubs and
grassland, with forested habitat making up approximately 10 percent (64.7 acres) of the project area.
Habitat surveys were completed in June of 2021 to determine if suitable habitat for Lewis's woodpeckers
was present in any of the parcels. No suitable habitat was identified for Lewis's woodpeckers in any of the
parcels. Nevertheless, surveys for this species were completed during the point-counts for migratory birds.
No Lewis's woodpeckers were detected. Given the lack of suitable habitat and the lack of detections, it is
assumed that this species is not present. No impacts are expected.
American three-toed Woodpecker
Suitable habitat for American three-toed woodpeckers (Picoides dorsalis) includes old-growth stands, areas
that have been recently burned, or areas that have numerous snags present. Densities of three-toed
woodpeckers appears to be higher in areas where there has been a recent fire or beetle infestation. They
prefer to nest in mature conifer forests that have had some sort of disturbance (UDWR 2021b).
Habitat surveys were completed in June of 2021 to determine if suitable habitat for three-toed woodpeckers
was present in any of the parcels. No suitable habitat was identified for three-toed woodpeckers in any of
the parcels. Nevertheless, surveys for this species were completed during the point-counts for migratory
birds. No American three-toed woodpeckers were detected. Given the lack of suitable habitat and the lack
of detections, it is assumed that this species is not present. No impacts are expected.

Cirrus Ecological Solutions, LC

20

Parleys Canyon Silver Mine — Resource Inventory and Reclamation Plan
Migratory Birds
Eight migratory birds of interest were identified as potentially using the project area (Brewer's sparrow
[Spizella breweri], long-billed curlew [Numenius americanus], olive-sided flycatcher [Contopus cooperi],
rufous hummingbird [Selasphorus rufus], Virginia's warbler [Vermivora virginiae], willow flycatcher
[Empidonax traillii], golden eagle [Aquila chrysaetosJ, and long-eared owl [Asio otus]). These species
utilize a wide range of habitat types for foraging and breeding that are found within the project area,
including forested habitat, shrubby habitat, and grasslands. Golden eagles and long-eared owls are
evaluated in separate sections below.
Due to the wide variety of habitat types that these species use, surveys were conducted to identify all birds
that might be present in each of the parcels. Point counts were conducted in April and May of 2021 to
identify any of the listed species that might be utilizing the area. Twelve point-counts were completed,
distributed in habitats throughout the project area. While many non-target bird species were observed
utilizing the parcels, none of the species of concern were detected.
It is possible these species utilize the area, as suitable habitat is present in the parcels. The Silver Mine
would disturb suitable habitat. Given the lack of detections and wide availability of suitable habitat in the
surrounding area, impacts would be limited, and would not result in any population-level effects.
Golden Eagle
Golden eagles utilize a wide variety of habitats, but primarily open habitat in hilly and mountainous areas.
Golden eagles can forage great distances in search of prey, including carrion. Nesting habitat is typically
cliff ledges or large trees, generally near grassland habitat. Nests are built of sticks and are very large, often
several feet wide and deep (Tesky 1994b).
Given the generalist nature of their foraging habitat, surveys were focused on identifying any suitable
breeding habitat or nests. There are areas within the project boundary that represent suitable golden eagle
nesting sites. These areas were surveyed by spotting scope and UAV in June of 2021. Figures 8 and 9 show
two potential golden eagle nests on cliffs in the project boundary. It is unknown how recently these nests
have been used, and while the construction and location of these nests indicates likely construction by
golden eagles, none were observed. Given the presence of these nests, it is likely that golden eagles are
utilizing, or have utilized, the project area as breeding and foraging habitat. Both nests are on the same band
of cliffs and are likely alternate nests used in the past by the same breeding pair.
Given the presence of likely golden eagle nests in the project area, but the unknown status of their recent
use, it is possible that one nesting pair of golden eagles could be impacted by the Silver Mine Project.
Should this occur, the breeding pair would need to use an alternate nest site as the existing nest site would
be destroyed by mining activities. Fortunately, there is ample cliff nesting habitat available within half a
mile or less to the east, west, and south. Due to the presence of alternate nesting sites outside of the project
area, it is unlikely that this project would result in any population-level effects.
Long-eared Owl
Long-eared owls breed in forested habitat where they utilize stick nests built by other birds, such as magpies
and crows. Suitable breeding habitat is always located adjacent to openings, meadows and fields that
provide suitable foraging habitat (Marks et al. 2020). The Silver Mine project area is predominantly shrubs
and grassland, with forested habitat making up approximately 10 percent (64.7 acres) of the project area.
Broadcast surveys were completed in suitable long-eared owl habitat in April of 2021. No long-eared owls
were detected during the broadcast surveys.
It is possible that this species may still utilize the project area for foraging, and the Silver Mine would
impact foraging habitat for long-eared owls. Given the lack of detections and wide availability of suitable
habitat in the surrounding area, impacts would be limited, and would not result in any population-level
effects.

Cirrus Ecological Solutions, LC

Fringed Myotis, Spotted Bat, and Townsend's Western Big-eared Bat
Fringed myotis (Myotis thysanodes) use a wide variety of habitat types, including forest edges and openings
(Keinath 2004). Spotted bats (Euderma maculatum) are found throughout the west in a variety of habitat
types, often near cliffs and permanent water (Priday and Luce 1999). Townsend's western big-eared bats
(Corynorhinus townsendii) roost in buildings, mines, and caves, and often forage along forest margins
(Kunz and Martin 1982).
Remote ultrasonic surveys were completed to identify foraging bats within the project area. Two ultrasonic
bat detectors were deployed in the mining boundary in July of 2021. None of these species were detected.
These three bat species are rare in northern Utah and a lack of detections at the site was expected.
It is possible that these species occasionally utilize the parcels as foraging habitat, and the Silver Mine
would impact foraging and roosting habitat, and possibly winter hibernacula considering the extensive
rocky outcrops in the project area. However, given the lack of detections and wide foraging range of these
three species, impacts would likely be limited, and would not likely result in any population-level effects.
Wildlife Habitat
The project area overlaps approximately 629.1 acres of wildlife habitat, with a mix of forested, shrubland,
and grassland and forb habitat present throughout the various parcels. Approximately 64.7 acres of forested
habitat, 355.4 acres of shrubland habitat, 104.5 acres of rocky outcrop, and 104.5 acres of grassland and
forb habitat are present in the project area. The loss of habitat would represent a small fraction of available
habitat in the surrounding area. Due to the large size of the project and the fairly narrow width of Parleys
Canyon, habitat connectivity would be somewhat disrupted for terrestrial species moving east or west up
or down the canyon. The movement path along the ridgetop would remain unimpacted. The species most
likely to be impacted by movement restriction caused by the presence of the Silver Mine would be large
home range big game species such as those discussed below. The level of impact this movement barrier
might have is difficult to determine without further data or study. Data from large-scale telemetry studies
conducted by the Utah Division of Wildlife Resources may provide insight into this question.
Biz Game
Five species of big game have overlapping ranges within the project area, black bear (Ursus americanus),
elk (Cervus canadensis), moose (Alces alces), mule deer (Odocoileus hemionus), and mountain lion (Puma
concolor) (Table 8).

Black Bear
Black bear utilize a wide variety of habitat types but are typically associated with forested habitat for
foraging and denning. The Silver Mine project area is predominantly shrubs and grassland, with forested
habitat making up approximately 10 percent (64.7 acres) of the project area. Home ranges for black bear
are often large, and female black bear can have overlapping home ranges. In the western states, black bear
average approximately 0.8 bears per square mile, although a low-density population in northern Utah was
found to be closer to 0.03 bears per square mile (UDWR 2011).
The large home range, elusive nature and wide-ranging movement patterns of black bear make surveying
for this species difficult. Therefore, no surveys were completed for this species. Bear scat was found near
a moose carcass in the project area indicating that the area is utilized by black bears. Due to the low
population density and large home ranges, impacts on black bears would be limited, and would not result
in any population-level effects.
Elk
Elk utilize higher elevation areas in the summer, typically in aspen conifer forests, and then move down to
mid and lower elevation areas in the winter. The Silver Mine project area is predominantly shrubs and
grassland, with forested habitat making up approximately 10 percent (64.7 acres) of the project area.
Approximately 612 acres of the project area overlaps with designated elk crucial winter habitat (UDWR
2021c). This overlap accounts for approximately 6 percent of the continuous designated crucial winter
habitat that surrounds the project area. Additionally, approximately 7 acres of substantial spring and fall
habitat overlaps with the southeastern corner of the project area (UDWR 2021c). This overlap accounts for
less than 1 percent of the continuous designated substantial spring and fall habitat that surrounds the project
area (Figure 10).

Anecdotal pellet surveys during April, May, and June showed substantial elk use in the project area.
Given the identification of pellets and designated winter habitat within the project area, habitat impacts
would be expected. Due to the size of the project and overlap with crucial winter habitat, effects from
habitat loss and/or habitat fragmentation could be measurable at the population level. The level of impact
this project might have is difficult to determine without further data or large-scale study. Data from largescale telemetry studies conducted by the Utah Division of Wildlife Resources may provide insight into this
question.
Moose
Suitable habitat for moose includes areas with shrubs and young trees for browsing, which often includes
river bottoms and riparian habitat. However, moose do not require these features, and can be found ranging
far from any obvious water source so long as browse is available. Moose often use dense conifer forests for
thermoregulation and shelter in the winter and the summer. The Silver Mine project area is predominantly
shrubs and grassland, with forested habitat making up approximately 10 percent (64.7 acres) of the project
area. The entirety of the project area overlaps with designated substantial winter habitat (UDWR 2021c).
This overlap accounts for approximately 2.5 percent of the continuous designated substantial winter habitat
the surrounds the project area (Figure 11).
Anecdotal pellet surveys during April, May, and June showed use by moose in the upper elevation portions
of the project area. Additionally, a moose carcass was located in the project area.

Figure 11. The Silver Mine project area in relation to crucial year-long and substantial winter
habitat for moose (UDWR 2021c).

Parleys Canyon Silver Mine — Resource Inventory and Reclamation Plan
Given the identification of pellets, a carcass, and designated substantial winter habitat within the project
area, habitat impacts would be expected. The level of impact this project might have is difficult to determine
without further data or large-scale study. Data from large-scale telemetry studies conducted by the Utah
Division of Wildlife Resources may provide insight into this question.
Mule Deer
Suitable habitat for mule deer includes a wide range of habitat types, but generally includes shrubs and
open areas used for foraging near forested habitat for cover and thermoregulation. The Silver Mine project
area is predominantly shrubs and grassland, with forested habitat making up approximately 10 percent (64.7
acres) of the project area. Similar to elk, mule deer also move from higher elevation areas in the summer to
mid and lower elevations in the winter. In Utah, most mule deer are migratory, travelling up to 70 miles
between their winter and summer ranges (UDWR 2019). Approximately 590 acres of the project area
overlaps with crucial spring and fall habitat for mule deer (UDWR 2021c). This overlap accounts for less
than 1 percent of the continuous crucial spring and fall habitat that surrounds the project area (Figure 12).

Mountain Lion
Mountain lion occupy a wide variety of habitat types, ranging from mountainous forested habitat to lower
elevation open habitat. Their choice of habitat tends to be driven by the type of prey they are targeting.
Home ranges of mountain lion can be very large, over 190 square miles in some areas (Tesky 1995).
The large home range, elusive nature and wide-ranging movement patterns of mountain lion make
surveying for this species difficult. Therefore, no surveys were completed for this species. It is assumed
that this species could utilize the project area. Given the small size of the project area compared to a single
mountain lion home range, impacts to mountain lions would be limited and would not result in any
population-level effects.

Ri )a rian Areas
Habitat surveys were completed to identify riparian habitat in any of the parcels. No riparian areas are
present.
Waterfowl and Riparian Areas
Habitat surveys were completed to identify aquatic habitat in any of the parcels that might provide habitat
for waterfowl. Waterfowl, with a few exceptions, prefer standing or slow-moving water for foraging and
resting. Some waterfowl species foraging exclusively in aquatic habitat, while others utilize water for
resting and forage mostly in upland habitat.
No riparian areas or aquatic habitat were identified in the project area. Given the lack of habitat, no impacts
are expected.

Minimization or Mitigation Measures for Wildlife Impacts
As discussed above, the impacts of the Silver Mine on most wildlife species are negligible. For species with
such impacts, no minimization or mitigation measures would be needed. For elk, moose, and mule deer
impacts due primarily to potential movement disruption could require mitigation if data from the Utah
Division of Wildlife Resources shows that significant movement corridors exist in the project area. A
variety of measures could apply, and the scale of these measures would be dependent upon the scale of the
impacts.

110.5 - REVEGETATION PLANTING PROGRAM AND TOPSOIL REDISTRIBUTION
Describe the revegetation tasks to be performed in detail, such as ripping, mulching, fertilizing, seeding
and scar0;ing of these areas. Correlate this information with the reclamation treatments map.
The following procedures have been designed to restore the vegetation community in the project area to
natural, pre-disturbance conditions as much as possible. As described previously, the floor of the mine and
bench surfaces are assumed to be reclaimed and would occur as described in the sections below.

Soil Material Replacement
In order to reestablish the required ground cover, one to two feet (depending on underlying material) of
suitable soil material usually has to be redistributed on the areas to be reseeded Describe the volume of
soils and approximate depth of soil cover to be used in reclamation. Describe the source of these soils and
provide an agronomic analysis of the soils. If there is insufficient soil, discuss alternate meansfor providing
adequate plant growth medium, such as amending subsoil overburden with biosolids or composted manure.
Describe the methods used to transport and place soils.
The total acreage of the mine floor and benches would be 143.5 and 240 acres, respectively. To provide
adequate planting material and ground cover, stockpiled topsoil berms would be re-distributed using large
Cirrus Ecological Solutions, LC

27

Parleys Canyon Silver Mine — Resource Inventory and Reclamation Plan
equipment to a minimum depth of 6 inches over the disturbed areas on the mine floor and benches. The
topsoil material would come from stockpiled topsoil removed from the site or hauled in from other sources,
if necessary. The depth of topsoil on the site varies widely with steeper slopes having shallower soil cover.
Based on final topography of the graded mine plan and plant requirements, approximately 308,146 cubic
yards of soil would be required during reclamation of the Silver Mine disturbed area.
The soil material used for reclamation should match the properties and qualities of the NRCS descriptions
for soil map units in the project area prior to disturbance, as much as practicable. Soil properties measured
from each soil unit are found in Table 9. Laboratory test results for pH, salinity, and sodium adsorption
ratio (SAR) indicate that soils meet the highest category of soil suitability (good) used to evaluate
reclamation potential (DOGM 2000). Measurements of soil texture (Table 3) and estimates of available
water holding capacity based on texture (USU Extension 2001) were also evaluated for reclamation
potential. Except for Site #4 (Unit 151) all results are classified in the good category for reclamation
potential. The soil sample in Unit 151 had a clay texture which is in the Fair and Poor categories for
reclamation potential (DOGM 2000). However, soil survey results indicate that Unit 151 has a gravelly
loam texture. Copies of laboratory test results are found in Appendix C.

Soil nutrients have an important influence on plant viability and revegetation success. Irrigated agriculture
and other cultivated areas use fertilizer to help seeds establish and improve crop production. However,
fertilizer use on arid rangelands is not recommended (USU Extension 2001). Fertilizer is beneficial only to
rangelands during years with high precipitation. During dry years, fertilizer applications on rangeland can
create problems with forage yields and plant vigor (NRCS 2000, AFRD 2003). Soil nutrients in the Silver
Mine area are lower than levels typically recommended for agricultural fields. However, based on the extent
and density of native species, nutrient levels are sufficient to sustain healthy vegetation communities.

Cirrus Ecological Solutions, LC
